"<br>---<br>   ¬ß¬ß A ((c:1;::gloss)) is ((c:2;::a brief explanation)), often ((c:3;::placed near the text)) ((c:2;::it is explaining)) ¬ß<br>   ¬ß¬ß An ((c:4;::interlinear gloss)) is ((c:5;::a gloss)) that is ((c:6;::placed between lines.)) ¬ß<br>   ¬ß¬ß In linguistics, ((c:7;::interlinear glossing)) follows the ((c:8;::Leipzig Glossing Rules)). ¬ß<br>   ¬ß¬ß In linguistics, ((c:9;::interlinear glossing following the Leipzig Glossing Rules)) is ((c:10;::morpheme))-by-((c:10;::morpheme)). ¬ß<br>   ¬ß¬ß However, since ((c:11;::the exact morphology)) is ((c:12;::not always clear)) or ((c:13;::may be at issue)), ((c:14;::interlinear glossing in linguistics)) is generally ((c:15;::for the readers understanding)), not ((c:16;::to state an analysis)). ¬ß<br> ===<br>  <section class=""cloze-group-children hide-if-inactive-children"">   Interlinear glossing according to ((c:17;::the Leipzig Glossing Rules)):   ¬ß¬ß Interlinear glosses are ((c:18;::aligned)) ((c:19;::word-by-word (not morpheme-by-morpheme!))), and in this way ((c:20;::left-aligned)). ¬ß<br>   ¬ß¬ß ((c:21;::Morphemes within the same word)) are ((c:22;::separated by hyphen)) both ((c:23;::within the object language)) and ((c:24;::in the gloss)). ¬ß<br>   ¬ß¬ß In Interlinear glosses, ((c:25;::grammatical morphemes)) can be rendered by ((c:26;::abbreviated lables (e.g. LOC))) or&nbsp; ¬ß<br>   ¬ß¬ß ((c:27;::by a word in the metalanguage (e.g. in))) ¬ß<br> In interlienar gloss, ((c:28;::the lables indicating grammatical morphemes)) are ((c:29;::written in caps)), most commonly ((c:30;::in small caps.))   ¬ß¬ß In interlinear gloss, the ((c:31;::most common)) of the abbreviated lables may be ((c:32;::further abbreviated)). ¬ß<br>   ¬ß¬ß ((c:33;::Clitic boundaries)) are marked by ((c:34;::an equals sign)). ¬ß<br>   ¬ß¬ß In interlinear gloss, if ((c:35;::one element in the object language)) is ((c:36;::rendered by several elements in the metalanguage)), those are ((c:37;::separated in the gloss by periods. (there are also optional further category separations here which use _ \ = ; &gt;)))  ¬ß<br>   ¬ß¬ß Things that are ((c:38;::contained in the gloss)) that are ((c:39;::not marked in the object language)) may either ((c:40;::be enclosed by [])) in ((c:40;::the metalanguage)), or ((c:41;::be indicated by √∏)) in ((c:41;::the object language)) ¬ß<br> </section>  <table>   <thead>     <tr>       <th>Grammatical Item</th>       <th>Abbreviation</th>     </tr>   </thead>   <tbody class=""cloze-group-children hide-if-inactive-children""> <tr><td>((c:47;::Article))</td> <td>((c:42;::ART))</td></tr> <tr><td>((c:43;::Demonstrative))</td> <td>((c:44;::DET))</td></tr> <tr><td>((c:45;::Honorific))</td> <td>((c:46;::HON))</td></tr>   </tbody> </table> <span class=""cloze-dump"">{{c1::}}{{c2::}}{{c3::}}{{c4::}}{{c5::}}{{c6::}}{{c7::}}{{c8::}}{{c9::}}{{c10::}}{{c11::}}{{c12::}}{{c13::}}{{c14::}}{{c15::}}{{c16::}}{{c17::}}{{c18::}}{{c19::}}{{c20::}}{{c21::}}{{c22::}}{{c23::}}{{c24::}}{{c25::}}{{c26::}}{{c27::}}{{c28::}}{{c29::}}{{c30::}}{{c31::}}{{c32::}}{{c33::}}{{c34::}}{{c35::}}{{c36::}}{{c37::}}{{c38::}}{{c39::}}{{c40::}}{{c41::}}</span><br><div class=""sub""> <table class=""blank-canvas cloze-group hide-if-inactive"" style=""text-align: left"">   <tbody>     <tr><td>My</td><td>s</td><td>Marko</td><td>poexa-l-i</td><td>avtobus-om</td><td>v</td><td>Peredelkino.</td></tr>     <tr> <td>1PL</td><td>COM</td><td>Marko</td><td>go-PST-PL</td><td>bus-INS</td><td>All</td><td>Peredelkino.</td></tr>     <tr><td colspan=""7"">'Marko and I went to Perdelkino by bus.'</td></tr>   </tbody> </table>  <table style=""text-align: left"" class=""blank-canvas cloze-group hide-if-inactive"">   <tbody>     <tr> <td>puer</td> <td>or:</td> <td>puer-√ò</td> </tr> <tr> <td>boy[NOM.SG]</td> <td></td> <td>boy-NOM.SG</td> </tr> <tr> <td>‚Äòboy‚Äô</td> <td></td> <td>‚Äòboy‚Äô</td> </tr>   </tbody> </table>   </div>"	acad::ughhh

"Which two features do both sounds spelled with the IPA with (a variant of) …ë share? <span class=""divider"">-&gt;</span> {{c1::open and back}}    What feature do all vowels share that are written with ligatures in IPA?  They are all front vowels Also either open, near open or open mid What do the IPA vowels written with an i/…™ all have in common, as it relates to height? "<div class=""cloze-group hide-if-inactive""><p>If an <span class=""c8-cloze"">argument</span> is <span class=""c2-cloze"">valid</span>, <span class=""c3-cloze"">the conclusion cannot be false</span> <span class=""c1-cloze"">if the premises are true</span>.</p> <p>For an <span class=""c7-cloze"">argument</span> to be <span class=""c4-cloze"">sound</span>, it must first <span class=""c5-cloze"">be valid</span>, in addition, <span class=""c6-cloze"">the premises must be true</span>.</p>   <p>An <span class=""c9-cloze"">argument</span> consists of <span class=""c10-cloze"">premises</span> and <span class=""c11-cloze"">conclusions</span>.</p>   <p>In an <span class=""c15-cloze"">argument</span>, the <span class=""c12-cloze"">truth of the premises</span> intends to <span class=""c13-cloze"">determine</span> <span class=""c14-cloze"">the truth of the conclusion</span>. </p>   <p>In a <span class=""c16-cloze"">deductive argument</span>, the t<span class=""c17-cloze"">ruth of the premises</span> <span class=""c18-cloze""><b>guarantees</b></span>&nbsp;<span class=""c19-cloze"">the truth of the conclusion</span>.</p> <p>An <span class=""c25-cloze"">argument</span> is the claim that given <span class=""c20-cloze"">every member of</span> <span class=""c21-cloze"">a certain premise set</span> <span class=""c22-cloze"">of formulae</span> is <span class=""c23-cloze"">true</span>, <span class=""c24-cloze"">so is the conclusion formula</span>.</p></div> <span class=""cloze-dump"">{{c1::}}{{c2::}}{{c3::}}{{c4::}}{{c5::}}{{c6::}}{{c7::}}{{c8::}}{{c9::}}{{c10::}}{{c11::}}{{c12::}}{{c13::}}{{c14::}}{{c15::}}{{c16::}}{{c17::}}{{c18::}}{{c19::}}{{c20::}}{{c21::}}{{c22::}}{{c23::}}{{c24::}}{{c25::}}</span>"

At its most general, parsing is taking strings and extracting information. In programming, parsing is often used for extracting a bit of useful data out of a string. In natural and computer languages, parsing takes a series of tokens and transforms them into some kind of data structure. In natural and computer languages, parsing is also called syntax/syntactic analysis. The data structure that parsing results in dependss on the input data. Parsing a CSV file may result in a list of records. Parsing natural or programming/markup/whatever languages often results in a tree. This tree is called a parse tree for programming/markup/whatever languages and syntax tree for natural language. The tokens for parsing/syntactic analysis in the lexical analysis sense are generated by lexical analysis/tokenization. After parsing/syntactic analysis comes semantic analysis. a parse tree is the result of {{c1::a derivation}} of a context-free grammar. {{c1::a syntax tree}} is a form of parse tree most common in linguistics. AST|Abstract syntax tree {{c1::}} more on ASTs, parse trees	acad::inf

"\{{c1::stackrel}}{{c2::{top}{bot} }} will {{c3::render the top text above the bottom text}} In Latex, there are a bunch of commands starting with \text (which I will call \textwhatever) that indicate different fontstyles: \textbf, \textit, \textrm (roman), \texttt (monospace), \textsc (smallcaps) If you try to type text in a math env as-is, it will look weird, as latex is formatting it for math. To insert text in a math environment, you can use any of \mathwhatever, \textwhatever, or just \text. It is generally advised that you use \text or \textwhatever within math environments when you want to write text, and \mathwhatever when you want to write math that just happens to be in roman letters. For many but not all \textwhatever font formatting commands, latex has corresponding \mathwhatever fonts (e.g. \mathrm, \math. The \mathwhatever fonts only work within a math environment. The \textwhatever fonts also work within a math environment. Within a math environment, there are some differences between \mathwhatever and \texthatever:  \mathwhatever uses the defined math font and \textwhatever uses the defined text font (which may be different) \mathwhatever does not preserve spaces within, but \textwhatever does. you can nest \textwhatevers but not \mathwhatevers. some text styles only exist as \mathwhatevers, e.g. mathfrak (Fraktur), mathbb (Blackboard bold)  In math environments, besides using \textbf or \mathbf, you can bold symbols by using \boldsymbol or \pmb 'poor man's bold' (which however only works by duplicating characters 3 times slightly offset)  frac{a}{b}   fraction (bruch)||<img src=""sm_JFBz6.png""> sqrt[root]{math}   square root (wurzel) \sum_lower^upper  There are also commands for math functions that are pure text (e.g. sin, lim), which have the advantage over just typing the characters that proper formatting is guaranteed  For commands (esp. math) that take something lower, it is often indicated {{c1::_{foo}}} For commands (esp. math) that take something upper, it is often indicated {{c1::^{foo}}}  \bar{foo}|ad a bar on top of letter <div class='c2-f'> What does this indicate? "Why will a tab freeze if you run code that takes very long?  because JS is single threaded and thus has to process the message to comopletion Why is the timeout we give setTimeout only a minimum time?  because its put at the end of the message queue and thus might take longer Why are we guaranteed that the things we are using from our function will not change 'behind our backs' while the function is running?  because we will always finish processing the curreent message (and thus the current stack contents) before we do anything else When the event loop processes a message in the message queue, what does it do?  calls the corresponding function What runs on JS's single thread hosts the event loop. The event loop processes the message queue Once the event loop has called the function of a message, when is it done?  when the call stack is empty Once the event loop has called the function of a message, what will it do with subsequent function calls within?  new stack frame on the stack In JS, stack frames are called execution contexts.  Each execution context contains a scope chain, which is a lists of lexical scopes from inner to outer. A lexical environment is a structure that holds identifiers and the variables/functions they refer to. What does setTimeout do with its function argument? (JS-internal view)  adds a new message with this function at the end of the message queue What does each  message in the message queue have associated with it?  a function Until the stack is empty, what can't we do?  start processing a new message In browsers generally each tab has  its own heap, stack and message queue If there's something on the stack, then what are we doing?  processing a message in the message queue How is JS threaded?  single-theaded Besides the heap and call stack, what does JS also have, as a core part of the implementation?  the message queue"	acad::js

Selecting Shadow Hosts from within a Shadow Tree A shadow host is outside of the shadow tree it hosts, and so would ordinarily be untargettable by any selectors evaluated in the context of the shadow tree (as selectors are limited to a single tree), but it is sometimes useful to be able to style it from inside the shadow tree context.  For the purpose of Selectors, a shadow host also appears in its shadow tree, with the contents of the shadow tree treated as its children. (In other words, the shadow host is treated as replacing the shadow root node.)  When considered within its own shadow trees, the shadow host is featureless. Only the :host, :host(), and :host-context() pseudo-classes are allowed to match it.  Why is the shadow host so weird? The shadow host lives outside the shadow tree, and its markup is in control of the page author, not the component author.  It would not be very good if a component used a particular class name internally in a shadow tree stylesheet, and the page author using the component accidentally also used the same class name and put it on the shadow host. Such a situation would result in accidental styling that is impossible for the component author to predict, and confusing for the page author to debug.  However, there are still some reasonable use-cases for letting a stylesheet in a shadow tree style its shadow host. (For example, the component might want to be laid out as a flexbox, requiring the shadow host to be set to display: flex.) So, to allow this situation but prevent accidental styling, the shadow host appears but is completely featureless and unselectable except through :host and its related functional forms, which make it very explicit when you‚Äôre trying to match against markup provided by the page author. How can you prevent FOUC with custom elements? <span class=""divider"">-&gt;</span> {{c1:::defined (and specifically :not(:defined))}} {{c1::custom elements}} are HTML elements that have their own {{c2::name}} and {{c3::custom functionality}}. The {{c1::Web Components::w...}} suite consists of {{c2::Custom elements}}, {{c3::Shadow DOM}} and {{c4::HTML templates}} <div class=""c2-f""> returns something of the type? </div><div class=""c1-f""> The most common and relevant element of this type is? </div><br>{{c1::window.customElements}}  <span class=""divider"">&lt;-&gt;</span> {{c2::CustomElementRegistry}} <div class=""c1-f""> When can you already use this? </div><br><pre><code>&lt;share-buttons&gt;   &lt;social-button type=""twitter""&gt;&lt;a href=""...""&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/social-button&gt;   &lt;social-button type=""fb""&gt;&lt;a href=""...""&gt;Facebook&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/social-button&gt;   &lt;social-button type=""plus""&gt;&lt;a href=""...""&gt;G+&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/social-button&gt; &lt;/share-buttons&gt; </code></pre> <span class=""divider"">-&gt;</span> {{c1::even before these custom elements are defined}}"	acad::inf

"a further {{c1::microinvalidation::one of the three types}} that {{c2::I'm suggesting}} is ""{{c3::denial of lived experience}}"" ({{c3::of x-ism}}) {{c1::Microaggressions}} are {{c2::brief}}, {{c2::everyday}} moments that {{c3::send denigrating messages}} to {{c4::stigmatized/marginalized group (the -value group of an ism)}}. While not explicitly one of the {{c1::8 themes}} of {{c2::microagressions}}, a further {{c3::microinvalidation}} that has been suggested is ""{{c4::denial of y-ism}}"", where {{c5::people deny the reality of the discrimination}} {{c1::DH Sue}} ({{c2::2007}}) laid out {{c3::8}} different {{c4::themes}} of {{c5::microagressions}}, which have {{c6::somewhat widely}} been used {{c1::DH Sue}} was mainly concerned with {{c2::race}}, but his {{c3::characterizations of microagressions}} have been also {{c4::been adopted in other discriminated-against(-value) communities}} {{c1::DW Sue}} ({{c2::2007}}), perhaps {{c3::the seminal work}} on {{c4::microagressions}}, describes {{c5::three typees}} of {{c4::microagressions}}: {{c6::Microinsult}}, which {{c9::demeans a person  (based on their marginalized indentity)}}, {{c7::Microassault}}, which {{c10::are designed to hurt the victim}}, and {{c8::Microinvalidations}}, which {{c11::nullify/negate the lived reality of a person}} One of the {{c1::8 microagressions}}, a type of {{c2::microinsult}} is ""{{c3::assumption of criminal status}}"", the presumption {{c4::that a person of the identity is criminal/dangerous/deviant}}. This is salient for {{c5::racism}}, but also strongly for {{c5::sanism/mentalism}}, and to a certain extent to {{c5::homophobia}} (and probably many others) One of the {{c1::8 themes}} of {{c2::microagressions}}, categorized as a {{c3::microinvalidation}}, are claims to be {{c4::color-blind}} ‚âà ""{{c5::denial of individual racism}}"", claiming in essence {{c6::that there might be racism, but I'm not doing it}}. Analogously, we might define {{c4::x-blind}} ({{c4::gender-blind, etc.}}) ‚âà ""{{c5::denial of individual y-ism}}"" claiming {{c6::that there might be y-ism, but I'm not doing it}}."	acad::ughhh

Proplog<br/><p class=""cloze-group hide-if-inactive""><span class=""c1-cloze"">Propositional logic</span> may also be called <span class=""c2-cloze c3-scr"">propositional calculus</span> or, based <span class=""c13-cloze"">represents</span> <span class=""c9-cloze"">any possible atomic proposition</span>.</span>   <span class=""line""><span class=""c10-cloze"">a propositional formula</span> (e.g. <span class=""c10-cloze"">p AND q</span>) (also called <span class=""c14-cloze c10-scr c11-scr c12-scr"">sentence (tho only in proplog)</span>) is a <span class=""c11-cloze"">well-formed formula</span> that <span class=""c12-cloze"">consists of elements of the alphabet of a propositional logic</span> (that is <span class=""c12-cloze"">propositional variables, constants, and logical connectives</span>).</span>   <span class=""line""><span class=""c15-cloze"">a proposition</span> <span class=""c16-cloze"">expressed by</span> <span class=""c17-cloze"">a propositional !acting  ## !families/schools/traditions  ### viewpoints  Broadly, ‚üÆc96;Viewpoints‚üØ ‚üÆcares less‚üØ about the ‚üÆpsychology of characters‚üØ and ‚üÆc-;more‚üØ about the ‚üÆsensory reality/space/perception/exeriences‚üØ  The main book on ‚üÆViewpoints‚üØ is ‚üÆThe Viewpoints Book‚üØ by ‚üÆAnne Bogart‚üØ and ‚üÆc_;Tina Landau.‚üØ  ‚üÆViewpoints‚üØ as a technique originates from ‚üÆdance‚üØ   #### soft focus  Ôºø‚üÆSoft focus‚üØÔºø is a technique from ‚üÆViewpoints‚üØ  Ôºø‚üÆSoft focus‚üØÔºø asks that we ‚üÆsoften our focus, looking at nothing in particular‚üØ  When we're in Ôºø‚üÆsoft focus‚üØÔºø, we able to ‚üÆpercieve  everything we can see‚üØ  When we're in Ôºø‚üÆsoft focus‚üØÔºø, rather than ‚üÆseeking out information‚üØ, ‚üÆinformation seeks us out ('penetrates' our sensiblities‚üØ)  Ôºø‚üÆSoft focus‚üØÔºø is contrasted with ‚üÆthe normal way of looking‚üØ, which is ‚üÆstaring at one particular thing (focus+saccades  or smooth pursuit movement‚üØ)   ### meisner  The Ôºø‚üÆc117;meisner technique‚üØÔºø says  ‚üÆyou should do something‚üØ only if ‚üÆsomething happens to make you do it‚üØ  The Ôºø‚üÆmeisner technique‚üØÔºø says ‚üÆwhat you do‚üØ ‚üÆdepends on the other fellow‚üØ  The Ôºømeisner techniqueÔºø emphasizes ‚üÆinstinctive, honest, authentic‚üØ reactions  Perhaps the most famous exercise of the Ôºø‚üÆmeisner technique‚üØÔºø is Ôºø‚üÆthe repetition‚üØÔºø  #### pinch and ouch  At its most literal, ¬ª‚üÆa pinch and ouch‚üØ¬´ in the ‚üÆmeisner‚üØ technique is ‚üÆsomeone pinching you, you react to that pain‚üØ  In the meisner Ôºøpinch and ouchÔºø, the ‚üÆpinch‚üØ generally is ‚üÆSomething that makes you feel a way‚üØ  In the meisner Ôºøpinch and ouchÔºø, the ‚üÆouch‚üØ is ‚üÆYour honest reaction‚üØ   # "# microeconomics  ¬ª‚üÆMicroeconomics‚üØ¬´ is the study of ‚üÆthe behavior and interactions of individual economic actors‚üØ ‚üÆwithin single markets‚üØ.  ## production  ¬ª‚üÆProduction‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆthe combination of various inputs‚üØ ‚üÆinto an output‚üØ. ¬ª‚üÆA production function‚üØ¬´ gives ‚üÆthe relation‚üØ between ‚üÆquantities‚üØ ‚üÆof Ôºøinputs‚üØÔºø and ‚üÆc-;quantities‚üØ ‚üÆof ÔºøoutputsÔºø.‚üØ  ### inputs  The ‚üÆ¬ªfactors of production¬´/¬ªinputs¬´‚üØ are ‚üÆwhat are necessary‚üØ to ‚üÆproduce the ÔºøoutputÔºø(s)‚üØ in Ôºø‚üÆproduction‚üØÔºø. ‚üÆfactor of production‚üØ ‚âà ‚üÆinput‚üØ  #### rent  ¬ª‚üÆRent‚üØ¬´ in economics is ‚üÆpayment‚üØ to ‚üÆa owner of‚üØ ‚üÆa Ôºøfactor of production/inputÔºø‚üØ in ‚üÆexcess of‚üØ what it costs ‚üÆc_-3;the owner‚üØ to ‚üÆbring‚üØ ‚üÆc_-4;the Ôºøfactor of production/inputÔºø‚üØ ‚üÆinto the ÔºøproductionÔºø process‚üØ. More specifically, economic ¬ª‚üÆrent‚üØ¬´ can be thought of ‚üÆany payment‚üØ for ‚üÆa Ôºøfactor of production/inputÔºø‚üØ that ‚üÆis itself not produced‚üØ, such as ‚üÆland or assets formed by creating official privilege over natural opportunities (e.g., patents)‚üØ.  ##### rent-seeking  ¬ª‚üÆRent-seeking‚üØ¬´ is the atttempt to ‚üÆgrow/maximize‚üØ ‚üÆeconomic ÔºørentÔºø‚üØ. Since ‚üÆcharging of rent does not increase productivity‚üØ, ‚üÆÔºørent-seekingÔºø‚üØ has the effect of ‚üÆincreasing one's share of wealth‚üØ without ‚üÆcontributing to productivity‚üØ.  ## costs and benefits  ### costs  A ¬ª‚üÆcost‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆthe value of money‚üØ that ‚üÆhas been used for something‚üØ, and thus ‚üÆis not available for use anymore‚üØ.  #### opportunity  When doing a certain thing, that thing's ‚üÆ¬ªopportunity cost¬´‚üØ is ‚üÆthe loss of value/benefit‚üØ from ‚üÆanything one could do instead (and has to forgo)‚üØ  #### related to the current situation  Related to the current situation, there may be ‚üÆÔºøsunk costsÔºø‚üØ and ‚üÆc_;Ôºøprospective costsÔºø‚üØ. A ‚üÆ¬ªsunk cost¬´‚üØ is a ÔºøcostÔºø that ‚üÆhas already been incurred and cannot be recovered‚üØ. a ‚üÆ¬ªprospective cost¬´‚üØ is a ÔºøcostÔºø that ‚üÆcan be avoided if action is taken‚üØ  #### related to production  flex-container:‚ú´240px-CVP-TC-FC-VC.svg.png‚ú´‚ú´Marginal_cost1a.png‚ú´   ‚üÆ¬ªFixed costs¬´‚üØ are ÔºøcostsÔºø that ‚üÆare not dependent on the quantity of Ôºøoutput‚üØÔºø. ‚üÆ¬ªvariable costs¬´‚üØ ‚üÆincrease in proportion to the Ôºøoutput‚üØÔºø. Together, ‚üÆÔºøfixed costsÔºø‚üØ and ‚üÆc_;Ôºøvariable costsÔºø‚üØ make up ‚üÆÔºøtotal costÔºø‚üØ ‚üÆ¬ªtotal cost¬´‚üØ is the ÔºøcostÔºø of ‚üÆproducing some quantity of ÔºøoutputÔºø‚üØ. The ‚üÆ¬ªmarginal cost¬´‚üØ of something is the ÔºøcostÔºø of ‚üÆproducing additional Ôºøoutput‚üØÔºø ‚üÆÔºøMarginal costÔºø‚üØ is either measured in ‚üÆone extra unit‚üØ, or in ‚üÆa graph for infinitesimal amounts‚üØ The ‚üÆÔºømarginal costÔºø‚üØ is calculated by ‚üÆdividing‚üØ ‚üÆŒî Ôºøtotal costÔºø‚üØ ‚üÆc-;by‚üØ ‚üÆŒî total quantity‚üØ The ‚üÆ¬ªaverage cost¬´‚üØ of something is ‚üÆthe Ôºøtotal costÔºø‚üØ ‚üÆdivided by‚üØ ‚üÆthe number of goods produced.‚üØ  ### externalities  An ¬ª‚üÆexternality‚üØ¬´ is a ‚üÆÔºøcostÔºø‚üØ or ‚üÆc_;benefit‚üØ ‚üÆcaused by a producer‚üØ that is ‚üÆnot incurred or received by that producer‚üØ A ‚üÆ¬ªnegative externality¬´‚üØ is an ÔºøexternalityÔºø that is ‚üÆa ÔºøcostÔºø‚üØ. A ‚üÆÔºøcommon-pool resourceÔºø‚üØ may be subject to ‚üÆthe Ôºøtragedy of the commonsÔºø‚üØ (however, not necessarily)  ####### tragedy of the commons  In ‚üÆ¬ªthe tragedy of the commons¬´‚üØ, ‚üÆany individual‚üØ has ‚üÆan incentive to‚üØ (over)exploit the common-pool resource. In the ‚üÆÔºøtragedy of the commonsÔºø‚üØ, the common-pool resource can't easily ‚üÆbe prevented from overexploitation‚üØ since it's ‚üÆsome degree of non-excludable‚üØ. In ‚üÆthe Ôºøtragedy of the commons‚üØÔºø, ‚üÆexploitation is problematic‚üØ since ‚üÆthe common-pool resource is rivalrous‚üØ. In ‚üÆthe Ôºøtragedy of the commonsÔºø‚üØ, the common-pool resource is ‚üÆdepleted‚üØ ‚üÆdue to the overexploitation. As proven by ‚üÆElinor Ostrom‚üØ in the work that won her ‚üÆthe 2009 economics nobel prize‚üØ, ‚üÆcommons/common-pool resources‚üØ can ‚üÆeffectively be managed by their communities‚üØ if ‚üÆcertain rules/principles are followed‚üØ, preventing ‚üÆthe tragedy of the commons‚üØ  ###### public goods  ¬ª‚üÆpublic good‚üØ¬´ is a good or service that is ‚üÆÔºønon-excludableÔºø‚üØ and ‚üÆÔºønon-rivalrousÔºø‚üØ Examples of ‚üÆÔºøpublic goodsÔºø‚üØ are ‚üÆNational security, lighthouses, knowledge‚üØ ‚üÆÔºøpublic goodsÔºø‚üØ are subject to ‚üÆÔºøfree rider problemsÔºø‚üØ  ####### free rider problem  ¬ª‚üÆFree riders‚üØ¬´ are people who ‚üÆuse a good or service‚üØ but ‚üÆdon't adequately contribute to the cost of its production.‚üØ In ¬ª‚üÆfree rider problems‚üØ¬´, ‚üÆÔºøfree ridersÔºø‚üØ result in ‚üÆthe good being under-produced or overused and degraded‚üØ. "# political spectrum  ## overton window  flex-container:‚ú´sm_paste-d320cd7f7b28b6755889aacc46fa08635d6a2262.jpg‚ú´   ¬ª‚üÆThe overton window‚üØ¬´ is the concept that there is ‚üÆa range of ideas/policies‚üØ that are ‚üÆacceptable‚üØ at ‚üÆany given time‚üØ, which then ‚üÆdetermines the acceptability/radicalness of other ideas‚üØ ‚üÆin relation to it‚üØ. "# tools  ## GIS  ‚üÆGIS‚üØ is short for ‚üÆgeographic information system‚üØ. "# general phenomena  ## reactions  ### basic terminology  A ¬ª‚üÆreagent‚üØ¬´ is a substance ‚üÆadded‚üØ ‚üÆto a system‚üØ to ‚üÆcause a chemical reaction‚üØ. ‚üÆA ¬ªreactant¬´‚üØ is ‚üÆa ÔºøreagentÔºø‚üØ that ‚üÆis consumed‚üØ ‚üÆin a chemical reaction‚üØ. ¬ª‚üÆProducts‚üØ¬´ are ‚üÆthe things‚üØ ‚üÆcreated in a chemical reaction‚üØ. The ¬ª‚üÆsubstrate‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆthe medium in which‚üØ ‚üÆa chemical reaction takes place‚üØ. The ¬ª‚üÆsubstrate‚üØ¬´ is also ‚üÆthe substance that is‚üØ ‚üÆtransformed in a reaction‚üØ. In biology, an ‚üÆ¬ªenzym substrate¬´‚üØ is a ‚üÆÔºøsubstrateÔºø‚üØ that is ‚üÆorganic material‚üØ ¬ª‚üÆSynthesis‚üØ¬´ in chemistry is ‚üÆperforming one or more chemical reactions‚üØ with the aim of ‚üÆconverting‚üØ ‚üÆany number of Ôºøreagents/reactantsÔºø‚üØ into ‚üÆany number of ÔºøproductsÔºø‚üØ. In general, ‚üÆÔºøsynthesisÔºø‚üØ produces ‚üÆmore complex compounds‚üØ as ‚üÆÔºøproductsÔºø (than its reagents/reactants‚üØ) ¬ª‚üÆBiosynthesis‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆ¬ªsynthesis¬´‚üØ ‚üÆwithin an organism‚üØ, using ‚üÆenzyms as catalysts‚üØ.  ## bonds  There are two general types of ‚üÆbonds‚üØ, ‚üÆintramolecular‚üØ and ‚üÆintermolecular‚üØ.  ### intramolecular  #### covalent   A ‚üÆ¬ªcovalent¬´ bond‚üØ is a ‚üÆintra-molecular bond‚üØ that involves the ‚üÆsharing‚üØ of ‚üÆelectron pairs‚üØ ‚üÆbetween atoms‚üØ.  # things  ## molecules  A ¬ª‚üÆmolecule‚üØ¬´ is a ‚üÆgroup of two or more‚üØ ‚üÆatoms‚üØ ‚üÆheld together‚üØ by ‚üÆintramolecular bonds‚üØ. Each ‚üÆshell‚üØ is composed of ‚üÆsubshells‚üØ Each ‚üÆsubshell‚üØ is composed of ‚üÆatomic orbitals‚üØ.   ##### shell numbers ‚Üî letters  table:Shell Number|Shell Letter ‚üÆ1‚üØ|‚üÆK‚üØ ‚üÆ2‚üØ|‚üÆL‚üØ ‚üÆ3‚üØ|‚üÆM‚üØ etc...|etc...  ##### electron contents per shell  The ‚üÆcloser the shell to the nucleus‚üØ, the ‚üÆsmaller its number / earlier to the alphabet its letter‚üØ identifier the ‚üÆnth‚üØ ‚üÆshell‚üØ can in principle hold up to ‚üÆ2(n‚é¥2‚é¥)‚üØ ‚üÆelectrons‚üØ.   table:Shell|Can hold this many electrons ‚üÆ1 / K‚üØ|‚üÆ2‚üØ ‚üÆ2 / L‚üØ|‚üÆ8 (2 + 6)‚üØ ‚üÆ3 / M‚üØ|‚üÆ18 (2 + 6 + 10)‚üØ etc...|etc...  #### valence electrons  A ‚üÆvalence electron‚üØ is an electron ‚üÆin the outer shell of an atom‚üØ  ## more than one category  ### radicals  A ‚üÆfree radical‚üØ is often just called a ‚üÆradical‚üØ. A ¬ª‚üÆ(free) radical‚üØ¬´ is a(n) ‚üÆatom/molecule/ion‚üØ that has ‚üÆan unpaired valence electron = unfilled valence shell‚üØ ‚üÆHaving an unpaired valence electron / unfilled valence shell‚üØ makes a ‚üÆÔºøfree radicalÔºø‚üØ ‚üÆhighly reactive‚üØ.  ### ions  An ‚üÆion‚üØ is a ‚üÆparticle, atom or molecule‚üØ with a ‚üÆnet electrical charge‚üØ. A ‚üÆcation‚üØ is a ‚üÆpositively‚üØ charged ‚üÆion‚üØ. "# theories ÔºÜ approaches  ## positivism  ¬ª‚üÆPositivism‚üØ¬´ as ‚üÆa sociological theory‚üØ holds that ‚üÆsociety is governed‚üØ ‚üÆaccording to general laws‚üØ  # social models  ## Social-Ecological Models  ‚üÆ¬ªSocial-ecolocgical models¬´‚üØ ‚üÆseparate‚üØ ‚üÆthings that can affect an individual‚üØ ‚üÆc-;into categories‚üØ.  The categories that ‚üÆsocial-ecological models separate things into‚üØ are ‚üÆnot always identical, though some are common.‚üØ  ### !The categories that social-ecological models separate things   onion-box: ‚üÆc+;s‚àû;Societal (Policy) ‚üØ   ‚üÆc+;s‚àû;Community‚üØ      ‚üÆc+;s‚àû;Organizational (more frequently left out) ‚üØ       ‚üÆc+;s‚àû;Relationship‚üØ          ‚üÆc+;s‚àû;Individual‚üØ  # units  ## community  ### community of practice  ‚üÆa ¬ªcommunity of practice¬´‚üØ is ‚üÆa set of people‚üØ who ‚üÆcome together‚üØ via ‚üÆmutual engagement‚üØ ‚üÆin a common endeavor‚üØ  In ‚üÆtheir engagement in their endeavor‚üØ, what emerges ‚üÆin a Ôºøcommunity of practiceÔºø‚üØ are ‚üÆWays of doing things, ways of talking, beliefs, values, power relations - in short, practices‚üØ   # demographics  ¬ª‚üÆDemography‚üØ¬´ is the statistical study of ‚üÆpopulations and poplulation change‚üØ   ## generations  table:Generation|Start|End|Nicknames ‚üÆBaby boomers‚üØ|‚üÆc+;s+;~1945‚üØ|‚üÆc+;s-;~1965‚üØ ‚üÆGeneration X‚üØ|‚üÆc+;s+;~1965‚üØ|‚üÆc+;s-;~1980‚üØ ‚üÆMillenials‚üØ|‚üÆc+;s+;~1980‚üØ|‚üÆc+;s-;~1995‚üØ|‚üÆc+;s‚àû;Generation Y‚üØ ‚üÆGeneration Z‚üØ|‚üÆc+;s+;~1995‚üØ|‚üÆc+;s-;~2010‚üØ|‚üÆc+;s‚àû;Zoomers‚üØ ‚üÆGeneration Œ±‚üØ|‚üÆc+;s+;~2010‚üØ|‚üÆc+;s-;???‚üØ  ## population pyramid  flex-container:‚ú´sm_world_population_2017.jpg‚ú´   A ‚üÆpopulation pyramid‚üØ indicates ‚üÆage brackets‚üØ on ‚üÆthe y axis‚üØ a ‚üÆpopulation pyramid‚üØ indicates ‚üÆamount of pepople‚üØ on ‚üÆthe x axis‚üØ A‚üÆ population pyramid‚üØ's indication of population is generally ‚üÆsplit into sex‚üØ  ## birth rate/fertility  The ‚üÆdifferent fertility rates are‚üØ sometimes presented ‚üÆas-is (as a percentage)‚üØ, or ‚üÆmultiplied by 1000 to ‚üÆpresent a number/index‚üØ   ### crude birth rate  What we often call ‚üÆbirth rate‚üØ is ‚üÆmore properly‚üØ called the ‚üÆÔºøcrude birth rateÔºø‚üØ,  The birth rade is ‚üÆmore properly called Ôºøcrude birth rateÔºø‚üØ because ‚üÆit isn't directly based on anything related to childbirth‚üØ  The ¬ª‚üÆcrude birth rate‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆthe number of live births‚üØ ‚üÆper person‚üØ  To get ‚üÆthe Ôºøcrude birth rateÔºø‚üØ in practice, we ‚üÆdivide‚üØ ‚üÆthe number of live births‚üØ ‚üÆc-;through‚üØ ‚üÆthe population size‚üØ  ### general fertility rate  The ¬ª‚üÆgeneral fertility rate‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆthe amount of live births‚üØ ‚üÆper women aged 15 to 44 (women of childbearing age)‚üØ   ### ASFR  The ¬ª‚üÆage-specific fertility rate‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆthe number of births‚üØ ‚üÆto women in a given age group‚üØ ‚üÆdivided by the number of women in a given age group‚üØ  The ‚üÆASFR‚üØ is often ‚üÆgrouped in groups of‚üØ ‚üÆ1‚üØ or ‚üÆc_;5‚üØ ‚üÆyears‚üØ.  In plain language, the ‚üÆASFR‚üØ e.g. for ‚üÆage 21‚üØ is ‚üÆthe amount of children an average 21 y/o has in a year‚üØ   ### TFR  The ‚üÆTFR‚üØ measures ‚üÆbabies per woman (in her lifetime)‚üØ (*)  Mathematically, the ‚üÆtotal fertility rate‚üØ is ‚üÆthe sum of all‚üØ ‚üÆcurrent age-specific fertility rates‚üØ  In plain language, the ‚üÆTFR‚üØ is ‚üÆthe sum of‚üØ ‚üÆthe amount of chilren‚üØ ‚üÆa women will have‚üØ ‚üÆin any given year of her life‚üØ  The thing the ‚üÆtotal fertility rate‚üØ is ‚üÆtrying to estimate‚üØ is ‚üÆthe amount of children a woman is expected to have in her lifetime (subject to some theoretical assumptions)‚üØ  #### caveats  The ‚üÆÔºøtotal fertility rateÔºø‚üØ assumes that ‚üÆthe woman will survive until the end of her reproductive life‚üØ and that ‚üÆshe will to experience the exact current age-specific fertility rates (ASFRs) through her lifetime‚üØ  If a ‚üÆwoman dies before her reproductive period ends (lets say at 30),‚üØ she ‚üÆwill not impact ASFRs beyond 30‚üØ. This is not a problem until ‚üÆwe add them together to the TFR‚üØ - since we are using the ASFRs to ‚üÆestimate how many children a woman will have in her life‚üØ, ‚üÆthe ASFRs‚üØ ‚üÆshould be impacted b‚üØ‚üÆy the woman who died before the end of the reproductive period (the dead women should count as 0‚üØ). Therefore, ‚üÆmaternal mortality distorts the TFR‚üØ.   #### replacement rate  The ¬ª‚üÆreplacement rate / replacement-level fertility‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆthe TFR‚üØ ‚üÆneeded to sustain population levels‚üØ  ¬ª‚üÆSub-replacement fertility‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆa TFR‚üØ ‚üÆbelow replacement rate‚üØ. Beyond ‚üÆthe distorting factors for the TFR‚üØ, ‚üÆthe Ôºøreplacement rateÔºø‚üØ is additionally distorted by ‚üÆchild mortality‚üØ.  ‚üÆThe Ôºøreplacement rateÔºø‚üØ for countries ‚üÆwith low child and maternal mortality‚üØ is ‚üÆabout 2.1‚üØ.   #### current TFRs  table:Country|TFR ‚üÆJapan‚üØ|‚üÆ~1.4‚üØ ‚üÆKorea‚üØ|‚üÆ~0.9‚üØ  ## dependency  ### definition of dependent  In demographics/economics, a ¬ª‚üÆdependent‚üØ¬´ is someone ‚üÆwho relies on someone else for financial support.‚üØ For statistical purposes, it is generally assumed that ‚üÆthose aged 15-64‚üØ are ‚üÆnon-dependents‚üØ For statistical purposes, it is generally assumed that ‚üÆthose aged 0-14 and 65-‚üØ are ‚üÆdependents‚üØ.  ### dependency ratio  The ¬ª‚üÆdependency ratio‚üØ¬´ is the ratio of of ‚üÆdependents per non-dependent‚üØ. The ‚üÆdependency ratio‚üØ is used as ‚üÆa proxy for‚üØ ‚üÆpressure on the productive population‚üØ, A ‚üÆlower‚üØ ‚üÆdependency ratio‚üØ is generally better because it often results in ‚üÆeconomic growth‚üØ.  #### demographic dividend/burdern  A ¬ª‚üÆdemographic dividend‚üØ¬´ is when a ‚üÆchange to a lower dependency ratio‚üØ ‚üÆleads to economic growth‚üØ Low dependency ratios (and thus demographic dividends) often happen when there is a ‚üÆhigh fertility rate‚üØ, resulting in ‚üÆa bunch of young people‚üØ, followed by ‚üÆa decrease in fertility‚üØ, and often ‚üÆaugmented by an increase in life expectancy‚üØ. ‚üÆChina's rapid economic growth‚üØ is often explained via the ‚üÆdemographic dividend‚üØ. in ‚üÆ2010‚üØ China had an unprecedented ‚üÆdependency ratio‚üØ of ‚üÆ35%‚üØ, at the a time where the world average was ‚üÆ53%‚üØ. A ¬ª‚üÆdemographic burden/tax‚üØ¬´ is when a ‚üÆchange to a higher dependency ratio‚üØ ‚üÆleads to economic stagnation‚üØ ‚üÆThe age group responsible for a low dependency ratio‚üØ ‚üÆaging out‚üØ results in ‚üÆa sudden high dependency ratio‚üØ. ‚üÆJapan‚üØ is the first country to really feel ‚üÆthe demographic tax/burden‚üØ. ‚üÆOther countries‚üØ such as ‚üÆcÔºøÔºõchina‚üØ ‚üÆwill soon begin feeling‚üØ ‚üÆthe demographic tax/burden‚üØ.  ## sex/gender  ### missing women  The term ‚üÆmissing women‚üØ was coined by ‚üÆAmartya Sen‚üØ. ¬ª‚üÆmissing women‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆthe shortfall‚üØ in ‚üÆthe number of women‚üØ relative to ‚üÆc_;the expected number of women‚üØ in ‚üÆa region or country‚üØ.  ‚üÆÔºømissing womenÔºø‚üØ are often measured/discovered through ‚üÆmale-to-female sex ratios‚üØ, specifically via ‚üÆthe deviance from‚üØ the ‚üÆnatural roughly 1:1 m:f ratio (in fact, in the west it is generally 0.98:1)‚üØ  ‚üÆSen‚üØ argued that there were about ‚üÆ100 million‚üØ ‚üÆÔºømissing womenÔºø‚üØ in ‚üÆAsia‚üØ.  The phenomenon of ‚üÆÔºømissing womenÔºø‚üØ and is theorized to be caused by ‚üÆsex-selective abortions‚üØ, ‚üÆfemale infanticide‚üØ, and ‚üÆinadequate healthcare and nutrition for female children‚üØ.  The fact of the existence of missing women is caused by ‚üÆsexism‚üØ, and may be seen as a pretty direct expression of ‚üÆtheir -value status.‚üØ   ## in ‚Äπplace‚Ä∫  ### japan "# sociolinguistics as a discipline  ¬ª‚üÆSocioloinguistics‚üØ¬´ is the study of ‚üÆthe interaction between‚üØ ‚üÆsociety and language‚üØ. ‚üÆÔºødialectologyÔºø‚üØ is ‚üÆa subfield of‚üØ ‚üÆsociolinguistics‚üØ. ¬ª‚üÆDialectology‚üØ¬´ is the study of ‚üÆvarieties‚üØ.  # variation  A ¬ª‚üÆvariant‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆa different ÔºølinguemeÔºø‚üØ expressing ‚üÆthe same ÔºøsememeÔºø/ÔºøpragmemeÔºø‚üØ.  A ¬ª‚üÆsociolinguistic variable‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆthe set of‚üØ ‚üÆÔºøvariantsÔºø‚üØ that ‚üÆmean the same thing‚üØ. ¬ª‚üÆVariation‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆthe fact of‚üØ ‚üÆmultiple variants existing (either in general, or for a specific situation)‚üØ ‚üÆExchanging‚üØ ‚üÆa ÔºøvariantÔºø‚üØ ‚üÆc-;for another‚üØ of ‚üÆthe same Ôºøsociolinguistic variable‚üØÔºø ‚üÆchanges‚üØ ‚üÆthe social meaning‚üØ, but ‚üÆc-;not‚üØ ‚üÆthe meaning meaning‚üØ.  ‚üÆvariationist sociolinguistics‚üØ is ‚üÆthe subfield of‚üØ ‚üÆsociolinguistics‚üØ that ‚üÆstudies sociolinguistiv variables specifically‚üØ.  # varieties  ‚üÆLect‚üØ = ‚üÆvariety‚üØ A ¬ª‚üÆvariety‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆa version of‚üØ ‚üÆa language‚üØ. A ‚üÆÔºøvarietyÔºø‚üØ ‚üÆlimits‚üØ ‚üÆthe ÔºøvariantsÔºø of Ôºøsociolinguistic variablesÔºø‚üØ. ^though it's not like any variant has exactly one variant for any sociolinguistic variable.  ## language  ‚üÆLanguage‚üØ in common discourse means ‚üÆstandard variety‚üØ. ‚üÆLanguage‚üØ in sociolinguistics means something like ‚üÆset of all relevant varietie‚üØs.  ## Languoid  ¬ª‚üÆLanguoid‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆa cover term‚üØ for ‚üÆlanguage‚üØ and ‚üÆÔºøvarietyÔºø‚üØ.  ## speech communities and varieties  A ‚üÆÔºøvarietyÔºø‚üØ is generally spoken by ‚üÆa certain Ôºøspeech communityÔºø‚üØ. There are ‚üÆspecific terms‚üØ for ‚üÆÔºøvarietiesÔºø‚üØ ‚üÆspoken by Ôºøcertain speech communitiesÔºø‚üØ.   table:type of variety|spoken by speech community defined by ¬ª‚üÆregister/style‚üØ¬´|‚üÆparticular communicative setting‚üØ ¬ª‚üÆideolect‚üØ¬´|‚üÆa specific person‚üØ ¬ª‚üÆsociolect‚üØ¬´|‚üÆa social group‚üØ ¬ª‚üÆc+;s‚àû;us+3;regiolect‚üØ¬´, ¬ª‚üÆc+;s‚àû;us+2;geolect‚üØ¬´, ¬ª‚üÆc+;s‚àû;us+;topolect‚üØ¬´|‚üÆa specific area‚üØ ¬ª‚üÆethnolect‚üØ¬´|‚üÆethnic group‚üØ   ‚üÆregister‚üØ and ‚üÆstyle‚üØ are synonyms. Since ‚üÆÔºøregisters/styleÔºø‚üØs are ‚üÆdefined by a situation‚üØ, not ‚üÆc_;a specific group‚üØ, these are also known as ‚üÆfunctional varieties‚üØ.  ## specific varieties  ### slang, jargon, cant, argot ...  ‚üÆslang‚üØ, ‚üÆjargon‚üØ, ‚üÆcant‚üØ, and ‚üÆargots‚üØ are all ‚üÆvarieties‚üØ. ‚üÆslang, jargon, cant, and argots‚üØ are all defined mainly by ‚üÆword choice‚üØ ‚üÆÔºøvariantsÔºø‚üØ. ‚üÆThe variants‚üØ that make up ‚üÆa certain‚üØ ‚üÆslang, jargon, cant or argot register‚üØ are words that have in common that ‚üÆthey are othwerise not in common usage‚üØ. It is hard to distinguish ‚üÆslang, jargon, argot, cant, etc.‚üØ because they are ‚üÆoften used interchangeably‚üØ ¬ª‚üÆSlang‚üØ¬´ is typically a ‚üÆÔºøregisterÔºø‚üØ that is ‚üÆinformal‚üØ and used in ‚üÆan in-group setting‚üØ. ¬ª‚üÆJargon‚üØ¬´ is typically the ‚üÆÔºøregisterÔºø‚üØ of ‚üÆa certain field‚üØ. ‚üÆCant‚üØ ‚âà ‚üÆargot‚üØ ¬ª‚üÆCant/argot‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆÔºøjargonÔºø‚üØ with the purpose to ‚üÆexclude or mislead people outside the field.‚üØ  ### AAVE  ‚üÆAAVE‚üØ = ‚üÆAfrican American Vernacular English‚üØ  # what is being varied?  ‚üÆPretty much any feature‚üØ can be ‚üÆÔºøvariedÔºø‚üØ to ‚üÆc_;create ÔºøvariantsÔºø‚üØ of ‚üÆc_;a Ôºøsociolinguistic variableÔºø‚üØ.  ## pronunciation  ### accent  An ¬ª‚üÆaccent‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆpronunciation‚üØ ‚üÆas a Ôºøsociolinguistic variableÔºø‚üØ.  ## ways of speaking  ### sprechbund  A ¬ª‚üÆspr‚Åëe‚Åëchbund‚üØ¬´ is a ‚üÆÔºøspeech communityÔºø‚üØ which has ‚üÆa shared way of speaking‚üØ ‚üÆgoing beyond language boundaries‚üØ ‚üÆas a ÔºøvariantÔºø‚üØ.  note that unlike a ‚üÆsprachbund‚üØ, a ‚üÆc_;spechbund‚üØ doesn't have to be ‚üÆgeographically contiguous‚üØ, and refers to ‚üÆspeech communities‚üØ, not ‚üÆc_;languages‚üØ, and is generally not ‚üÆabout vocabulary or grammar per se‚üØ, but ‚üÆc_;more about a more abstract manner‚üØ A ‚üÆÔºøsprechbundÔºø‚üØ might e.g. be ‚üÆhip-hop with the characteristics of rapping, rhyme, and human beatboxing‚üØ.  # variation in different situations  ## accomodation  Speech Acommodation Theory = Communication Accomodation Theory ¬ª‚üÆSpeech/Communication Accommodation Theory‚üØ¬´ studies ‚üÆÔºøacommodationÔºø‚üØ and thus ‚üÆc_;Ôºøconvergence/divergenceÔºø‚üØ. ¬ª‚üÆAccomodation‚üØ¬´ is the process where ‚üÆparticipants‚üØ ‚üÆchange their way of speaking‚üØ according to ‚üÆc-;the way of speaking‚üØ of ‚üÆother participants in the conversation‚üØ ‚üÆÔºøAcommodationÔºø (CAT)‚üØ may be ‚üÆÔºøconvergenceÔºø‚üØ or ‚üÆc_;ÔºødivergenceÔºø‚üØ. ¬ª‚üÆdivergence‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆÔºøaccomodationÔºø‚üØ by ‚üÆmaking your speech more ‚Åëdis‚Åësimilar to the conversational partner's‚üØ ¬ª‚üÆconvergence‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆÔºøaccomodationÔºø‚üØ by ‚üÆmaking your speech more similar to the conversational partner's‚üØ  ## A ¬ª‚üÆfocused speech community‚üØ¬´ is one where ‚üÆthere is agreement‚üØ about ‚üÆthe shared languoid's features‚üØ A ¬ª‚üÆdiffuse speech community‚üØ¬´ is one where ‚üÆthere is less/little agreement‚üØ about ‚üÆthe shared languoid's features‚üØ ‚üÆEach person‚üØ belongs to ‚üÆmany‚üØ ‚üÆÔºøspeech communitiesÔºø‚üØ.  ## diglossia   ¬ª‚üÆDiglossia‚üØ¬´ is the situation where ‚üÆa speech community‚üØ uses ‚üÆtwo (or more)‚üØ ‚üÆÔºølanguoidsÔºø‚üØ ‚üÆfor separate purposes‚üØ. ‚üÆvernacular variety‚üØ = ‚üÆL variety‚üØ ‚üÆhigh/formal/literary variety‚üØ = ‚üÆH variety‚üØ For ‚üÆÔºødiglossiaÔºø‚üØ usually ‚üÆa L variety‚üØ and ‚üÆa H variety‚üØ exist   table:variety|used in|native speakers ‚üÆL‚üØ|‚üÆeveryday situations‚üØ|‚üÆnative speakers‚üØ ‚üÆH‚üØ|‚üÆformal situations, literature‚üØ|‚üÆno native speakers‚üØ   ‚üÆ„ÄÄÊñáË™ûÔºà„Å∂„Çì„ÅîÔºâ (classical japanese)‚üØ was ‚üÆa H variety‚üØ during ‚üÆÔºødiglossiaÔºø‚üØ.  # the sociolinguistic situation of certain groups  ## language and gender  ### history  The study of ‚üÆlanguage and gender‚üØ is said to ‚üÆhave stared with‚üØ „Äé‚üÆLanguage and Women's Place‚üØ„Äè „Äé‚üÆLanguage and Women's Place‚üØ„Äè was written by ‚üÆRobin Lakoff‚üØ in ‚üÆ1973 or 1975, its unclear to me‚üØ. ‚üÆRobin Lakoff‚üØ was ‚üÆmarried to‚üØ ‚üÆthe (arguably) more famous Lakoff (of Metaphors We Live By fame)‚üØ ‚üÆDeborah Tannen‚üØ was ‚üÆa student of‚üØ ‚üÆRobin Lakoff‚üØ  ‚üÆDeborah Tannen‚üØ wrote „Äé‚üÆYou Just Don't Understand‚üØ„Äè in ‚üÆ1990‚üØ. „Äé‚üÆYou Just Don't Understand‚üØ„Äè ‚üÆinitiated the difference approach‚üØ to language and gender.  ### approaches  There are broadly ‚üÆthree‚üØ approaches to ‚üÆexplain differences between‚üØ ‚üÆmale and female language‚üØ. ‚üÆThe three approaches to explain the differences between male and female language‚üØ are the ‚üÆÔºødominanceÔºø‚üØ, ‚üÆÔºødeficitÔºø‚üØ and ‚üÆÔºødifferenceÔºø‚üØ approach. The ‚üÆÔºødeficitÔºø‚üØ and ‚üÆc_;ÔºødominanceÔºø‚üØ approach ‚üÆhave in common‚üØ that they ‚üÆassert that women's language is weaker than men's language‚üØ. The ¬ª‚üÆdeficit approach‚üØ¬´ argues that ‚üÆwomen's language being weaker‚üØ ‚üÆcauses/perpetuates‚üØ ‚üÆwomen's oppression‚üØ. The ¬ª‚üÆdominance approach‚üØ¬´ argues that ‚üÆwomen's language being weaker‚üØ ‚üÆis a reflection of‚üØ ‚üÆwomen's oppression‚üØ. The ¬ª‚üÆdifference approach‚üØ¬´ asserts that ‚üÆwomen's language‚üØ ‚üÆis not weaker or worse‚üØ, instead ‚üÆc_;just different from‚üØ ‚üÆc-;men's language‚üØ.  ### gender in words  ‚üÆWords may have gender‚üØ in roughly ‚üÆfour‚üØ ways. ‚üÆThe four ways in which words may have gender‚üØ are ‚üÆlexical gender‚üØ, ‚üÆgrammatical gender‚üØ, ‚üÆreferential gender‚üØ, ‚üÆsocial gender‚üØ. ‚üÆlexical, grammatical, referential and social gender‚üØ may ‚üÆcontradict‚üØ each other.  ¬ª‚üÆlexical gender‚üØ¬´ is gender ‚üÆas inherent in the reference of words‚üØ. ‚üÆÔºølexical genderÔºø‚üØ is ‚üÆmost common‚üØ in ‚üÆkinship terms‚üØ Examples for ‚üÆÔºølexically genderedÔºø‚üØ words: ‚üÆMother, aunt, son, father‚üØ ¬ª‚üÆsocial gender‚üØ¬´ is words being gendered ‚üÆvia their gender connotations‚üØ Examples for ‚üÆÔºøsocial genderedÔºø‚üØ words: ‚üÆsurgeon, lawyer is pronominalized by he even when gender is unknown‚üØ ¬ª‚üÆreferential gender‚üØ¬´ is words being gendered ‚üÆvia the gender of their deictic referent‚üØ  #### M√§dchen f√ºr alles  !If a male person is called a ""M√§dchen f√ºr alles"", M√§dchen has...   table:gender|value ‚üÆlexical gender‚üØ|‚üÆfemale (girl)‚üØ ‚üÆgrammatical gender‚üØ|‚üÆneuter (das M√§dchen)‚üØ ‚üÆreferential gender‚üØ|‚üÆmale (the deictic referent.)‚üØ  # how does variation interact with other forces?  ## standartization  ‚üÆStandard variety‚üØ ‚âà ‚üÆstandard language‚üØ. !A ¬ª‚üÆstandard variety‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆa certain ÔºøvarietyÔºø‚üØ of a language with two properties: - !‚üÆstandartized to a certain extent‚üØ - !‚üÆthought to be/promoted as correct‚üØ  ### how many standard varieties? "# typography  ¬ª‚üÆTypography‚üØ¬´ is the field concerned with ‚üÆthe properties/use of‚üØ ‚üÆtype and text (in a wide sense)‚üØ ‚üÆÔºøTypographyÔºø‚üØ is divided into ‚üÆÔºømicrotypographyÔºø‚üØ and ‚üÆc_;ÔºømacrotypographyÔºø‚üØ.  ¬ª‚üÆMicrotypography‚üØ¬´ is typography on the level of ‚üÆletters/words‚üØ Examples of ‚üÆÔºømicrotypographicÔºø‚üØ properties/concerns are ‚üÆletter spacing, word spacing, ligatures, features of letters‚üØ ¬ª‚üÆMacrotypography‚üØ¬´ is typography on the level of ‚üÆthe collumn/grid/page‚üØ Examples of ‚üÆÔºømacrotypographicÔºø‚üØ properties/concerns are ‚üÆTypographic hierarchy, typographic grid, page layout‚üØ  <span class=""cloze-dump"">{{c1::}}{{c2::}}{{c3::}}{{c4::}}{{c5::}}{{c6::}}{{c7::}}{{c8::}}{{c9::}}{{c10::}}{{c11::}}{{c12::}}{{c13::}}{{c14::}}</span>"	linguistics::graphemics::typography
"# microtypography  flex-container:‚ú´sm_tmprdkiv_z1.jpg‚ú´  ## fonts and font families  A synonym of ‚üÆtypeface‚üØ is ‚üÆfont family‚üØ. The ¬ª‚üÆen/em space/dash‚üØ¬´ are ‚üÆa space and dash‚üØ that are defined as ‚üÆone en/em‚üØ ‚üÆwide‚üØ. The ‚üÆen/em space‚üØ and ‚üÆc_;en/em dash‚üØ are ‚üÆone en/em‚üØ ‚üÆwide‚üØ.  Due to ‚üÆthe confusion about definitions of en/ems‚üØ, sometimes ‚üÆvariant definitions‚üØ for ‚üÆen/em dashes/spaces‚üØ are used.   #### point  In typography, the ¬ª‚üÆpoint‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆthe smallest‚üØ ‚üÆunit of measure‚üØ.  ‚üÆthe exact size‚üØ of ‚üÆthe ÔºøpointÔºø‚üØ ‚üÆhas varied‚üØ. ‚üÆÔºøpointÔºø‚üØ is often abbreviated ‚üÆpt‚üØ or ‚üÆc_;just p‚üØ.  ¬ª‚üÆA pica‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆ12 point (whatever point you're using)‚üØ.  ##### DTP point  ‚üÆDTP point‚üØ = ‚üÆDeskTop Publishing point‚üØ. the ‚üÆÔºøDTP pointÔºø‚üØ ‚üÆcame into existence‚üØ ‚üÆwith digital printing‚üØ. The ‚üÆÔºøDTP pointÔºø‚üØ has become a sort of ‚üÆstandard ÔºøpointÔºø‚üØ. the ¬ª‚üÆDTP point‚üØ¬´ is defined as ‚üÆ1/72 inch‚üØ. The ‚üÆÔºøbody heightÔºø‚üØ when talking about ‚üÆmovable type‚üØ is ‚üÆthe height of the sort‚üØ.   #### baseline, mean line  The ¬ª‚üÆbaseline‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆthe imaginary line‚üØ ‚üÆon which most letters sit‚üØ. The ¬ª‚üÆmean line‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆthe imaginary line‚üØ ‚üÆup to which most lowercase letters extend‚üØ.  #### x-height  ‚üÆx-height‚üØ = ‚üÆcorpus size‚üØ The ¬ª‚üÆx-height‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆthe distance between‚üØ ‚üÆÔºøbaselineÔºø and Ôºømean lineÔºø‚üØ. ‚üÆthe height of the x-height‚üØ typically ‚üÆcorresponds to the height of the x‚üØ, ‚üÆhence the name‚üØ.   #### overshoot  ¬ª‚üÆOvershoot‚üØ¬´ is when letters ‚üÆextend a little‚üØ ‚üÆabove or below‚üØ ‚üÆthe lines meant to constrain them‚üØ. Letters such as ‚üÆlowercase f, h and uppercase letters (if we want to count them‚üØ) typically have ‚üÆÔºøascendersÔºø‚üØ.  The ¬ª‚üÆascender height‚üØ¬´ is the ‚üÆdistance‚üØ that ‚üÆÔºøascendersÔºø reach above the ÔºøbaselineÔºø‚üØ.  The ‚üÆÔºøascender heightÔºø‚üØ is generally ‚üÆhigher than‚üØ ‚üÆthe Ôºøcap-heightÔºø‚üØ  ‚üÆascender line‚üØ = ‚üÆtopline‚üØ The ¬ª‚üÆascender/descender line‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆthe imaginary line‚üØ ‚üÆdefined by‚üØ ‚üÆthe Ôºøascender height/descender depthÔºø‚üØ  ‚üÆLine spacing‚üØ = ‚üÆleading‚üØ ¬ª‚üÆLine spacing‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆthe distance between‚üØ ‚üÆthe Ôºødescender lineÔºø‚üØ and ‚üÆthe next Ôºøascender lineÔºø‚üØ   #### super/subscript  ‚üÆÔºøSubscriptÔºø and ÔºøsupersciptÔºø‚üØ text is usually ‚üÆsmaller than‚üØ regular text.  ¬ª‚üÆSuperscript‚üØ¬´ text is text whose ‚üÆÔºøbaselineÔºø‚üØ ‚üÆsits somewhat above‚üØ ‚üÆc-;the general ÔºøbaselineÔºø‚üØ.  ¬ª‚üÆSubscript‚üØ¬´ text may ‚üÆsit at the general ÔºøbaselineÔºø‚üØ and ‚üÆjust be smaller‚üØ or ‚üÆhave a ÔºøbaselineÔºø‚üØ ‚üÆsomewhat below‚üØ ‚üÆc-;the general ÔºøbaselineÔºø‚üØ.   #### em square ÔºÜ relative sizing  flex-container:‚ú´220px-Metal_type.svg.png‚ú´  The ¬ª‚üÆem square‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆan imaginary square‚üØ ‚üÆaround which parts of letters are sized‚üØ. The ‚üÆÔºøem squareÔºø‚üØ is used in ‚üÆdigital typography‚üØ. The ‚üÆÔºøem squareÔºø‚üØ is ‚üÆ1em‚üØ ‚üÆwide and high‚üØ. Using ‚üÆÔºøthe em sqareÔºø‚üØ allows us to ‚üÆgive the size‚üØ of ‚üÆany other part of the font (e.g. ascender height, x-height, cap height, etc. etc.)‚üØ ‚üÆin em‚üØ. The size relationship ‚üÆbetween parts of letters‚üØ ofc. depends on ‚üÆthe typeface‚üØ.  ##### average relative sizing  table:type property|size ‚üÆcap height‚üØ|‚üÆ0.7em‚üØ ‚üÆx-height‚üØ|‚üÆ0.5em‚üØ  ### font properties  #### serifs  Serifs are these little bits sticking out of some letters. One major axis along which font families are distinguished is if they have serifs or not.  #### monospace  A monospaced font where all characters occupy the same horizontal space. A font that is not monospaced is proportional-width.  ### installation  ‚üÆinstalling custom font families‚üØ is possible on ‚üÆmost desktop os‚üØ, but not on ‚üÆandroid‚üØ (and ofc not on ‚üÆios‚üØ) "# macrotypography  ## columns  A ¬ª‚üÆcolumn‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆ(a) vertical block(s) of content‚üØ ‚üÆpositioned on a page‚üØ. ‚üÆÔºøColumnnsÔºø‚üØ and ‚üÆc_;rows‚üØ are ‚üÆseparated by‚üØ ‚üÆÔºøguttersÔºø‚üØ. ¬ª‚üÆGutters‚üØ¬´ are ‚üÆwhitespace‚üØ ‚üÆbetween‚üØ ‚üÆtwo rows or columns‚üØ.  ## page  ### page layout  #### spacing  ##### margins  flex-container:‚ú´220px-Margins.svg.png‚ú´   the ‚üÆmargin‚üØ is ‚üÆthe area between‚üØ ‚üÆthe main content of a page‚üØ and ‚üÆits edges‚üØ.  ##### alignment ÔºÜ indentation  flex-container:‚ú´https://barbarakristaponis.files.wordpress.com/2015/11/dividequote1-e1447790290375.png?w=400‚ú´   ¬ª‚üÆx-aligned‚üØ¬´ text is where ‚üÆa line‚üØ w‚üÆill always start‚üØ at ‚üÆthe x side (or at a certain offset)‚üØ ¬ª‚üÆA rag‚üØ¬´ is an ‚üÆuneven‚üØ ‚üÆmargin‚üØ. ‚üÆÔºøx-alignedÔºø‚üØ text will have ‚üÆÔºøragsÔºø‚üØ on Ôºøthe opposite sideÔºø. Text being ¬ª‚üÆflush (with) x‚üØ¬´ is when the text ‚üÆtouches the x side‚üØ. ¬ª‚üÆIndentation‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆempty space‚üØ at ‚üÆthe beginning of the line‚üØ so ‚üÆit is no longer flush‚üØ ¬ª‚üÆA hanging indent‚üØ¬´ is one way in which a paragraph might be ‚üÆx-aligned‚üØ but not ‚üÆcompletely flush x‚üØ. Using a ‚üÆÔºøhanging indentÔºø‚üØ, ‚üÆthe first line‚üØ is ‚üÆflush x‚üØ and ‚üÆc-;subsequented lines‚üØ are ‚üÆindented‚üØ. If ‚üÆthe note seciton is placed at the end of the chapter/paper/whatever‚üØ, ‚üÆÔºøthe notesÔºø‚üØ are known as ¬ª‚üÆendnotes‚üØ¬´.  ## multiple pages  ### division  #### pagination  ‚üÆpagination‚üØ = ‚üÆpaging‚üØ ¬ª‚üÆpagination‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆdividing‚üØ ‚üÆa given document‚üØ ‚üÆc_-;into‚üØ ‚üÆdiscrete pages‚üØ.  ### relationships between pages  #### orphans and widows  flex-container:‚ú´sm_BdflVyb4vu7o0q5hCeBkmu09nMn21opE.jpg‚ú´   ¬ª‚üÆWidows/orphans‚üØ¬´ refer to ‚üÆstranded things‚üØ at ‚üÆthe beginning/end of‚üØ ‚üÆother things‚üØ.   !¬ª‚üÆWidows/orphans‚üØ¬´ may either refer to - !stranded ‚üÆwords‚üØ at the beginning/end of ‚üÆc_;paragraphs‚üØ - !stranded ‚üÆlines‚üØ at the beginning/end of ‚üÆc_;pages‚üØ. In ‚üÆall books‚üØ, the ‚üÆfirst page‚üØ is ‚üÆthe cover‚üØ. in ‚üÆLtR media‚üØ, the ‚üÆsecond page‚üØ will be ‚üÆa left page‚üØ Since in ‚üÆLtR media‚üØ, ‚üÆthe second page will be a left page‚üØ, ‚üÆthe cover is a right page‚üØ in ‚üÆRtL media‚üØ, the ‚üÆsecond page‚üØ will be ‚üÆa right page‚üØ Since in ‚üÆRtL media‚üØ, ‚üÆthe second page will be a right page‚üØ, ‚üÆthe cover is a left page‚üØ  ##### twosided  In a book, ‚üÆleft and right pages‚üØ are ‚üÆnot formatted identical‚üØ, but instead ‚üÆhave different e.g. margins‚üØ to ‚üÆaccount for binding‚üØ etc. ‚üÆThe state of left and right pages not being identical‚üØ may be called ¬ª‚üÆtwo-sided‚üØ¬´. "# types of graphemes  typically, ‚üÆwhatevergraph‚üØ and ‚üÆwhatevergram‚üØ are ‚üÆsynonyms‚üØ.  ## graphs with phonological realization ‚âà representing basic linguistic unit  ### logograms vs phonograms  the two types of ‚üÆgrapheme‚üØ ‚üÆrepresenting a basic linguistic unit‚üØ are ‚üÆÔºølogogramsÔºø‚üØ and ‚üÆÔºøphonogramsÔºø‚üØ ¬ª‚üÆa logogram ‚üØ¬´is a ‚üÆgraph/grapheme‚üØ representing ‚üÆa morpheme/word‚üØ ¬ª‚üÆa phonogram‚üØ¬´ is a ‚üÆgraph/grapheme‚üØ representing ‚üÆone or more phonemes‚üØ  ### logograms  Well-known ‚üÆÔºølogographicÔºø‚üØ writing systems are ‚üÆkanji/hanzi/hanja/han tu (though there are exceptions); hieroglyphs‚üØ Depending on their composition, ‚üÆÔºølogogramsÔºø‚üØ may be ‚üÆÔºønon-compoundÔºø‚üØ or ‚üÆÔºøcompoundÔºø‚üØ. ¬ª‚üÆCompound logograms‚üØ¬´ are ‚üÆÔºølogogramsÔºø‚üØ ‚üÆconsisting of multiple other logograms‚üØ. ¬ªNon-compound logograms¬´ are ‚üÆÔºølogogramsÔºø‚üØ ‚üÆconsisting of no other logograms‚üØ.  #### non-compound logograms  ‚üÆÔºøNon-compound logogramsÔºø‚üØ may be ‚üÆÔºøpictogramsÔºø‚üØ or ‚üÆÔºøideogramsÔºø‚üØ. ¬ª‚üÆA pictogram‚üØ¬´ ‚üÆdepicts the thing‚üØ it ‚üÆreferences‚üØ, or ‚üÆonce did‚üØ. Examples of ‚üÆÔºøpictogramÔºø‚üØ kanji: ‚üÆÊó•, Êúà, Â±±, Êú®, Âè£ etc.‚üØ ¬ª‚üÆAn ideogram‚üØ¬´ ‚üÆattempts to visualize‚üØ ‚üÆthe abstract concept‚üØ it ‚üÆrepresents‚üØ. Many ‚üÆÔºøideogramsÔºø‚üØ are ‚üÆderived from‚üØ ‚üÆÔºøpictogramsÔºø‚üØ.  #### compound logograms  some ‚üÆcompound logograms‚üØ may ‚üÆfunction similar to ideograms or pictograms‚üØ, but are ‚üÆrarely called such‚üØ due to the differences.  ### phonograms  ‚üÆÔºøphonograpmsÔºø‚üØ are divided into ‚üÆÔºøsyllabogramsÔºø‚üØ and ‚üÆÔºølettersÔºø‚üØ. ¬ª‚üÆA syllabogram‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆÔºøa graph/graphemeÔºø‚üØ that (ideally) ‚üÆrepresents a syllable or mora‚üØ. ¬ª‚üÆA letter‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆÔºøa graph/graphemeÔºø‚üØ that (ideally) ‚üÆrepresents a single phoneme‚üØ. A ¬ª‚üÆsyllabary‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆÔºøa writing systemÔºø‚üØ that consists of ‚üÆÔºøsyllabogramsÔºø‚üØ. An ¬ª‚üÆalphabet‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆÔºøa writing systemÔºø‚üØ that consists of ‚üÆÔºølettersÔºø‚üØ.  #### hybrids  I will call the ‚üÆcharacters of an abugida‚üØ ‚üÆÔºøabugidogramÔºø‚üØ, though ‚üÆthis is a term no one uses‚üØ. ¬ª‚üÆAn abugidogram‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆa graph/grapheme‚üØ which ‚üÆrepresents a consonant‚üØ, and ‚üÆc_;vowels‚üØ are ‚üÆadded as modifications of the base character‚üØ.  An ¬ª‚üÆabjad‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆÔºøa writing systemÔºø‚üØ which ‚üÆprimarily deals in consonants‚üØ. A ¬ª‚üÆpure abjad‚üØ¬´ has ‚üÆno way of indicating vowels whatsoever‚üØ. An ¬ª‚üÆimpure abjad‚üØ¬´ has ‚üÆsome way of indicating vowels‚üØ. An ‚üÆÔºøimpure abjadÔºø‚üØ may ‚üÆindicate vowels‚üØ with ‚üÆdiacritics‚üØ or ‚üÆa limited set of vowel letters‚üØ.  ## graphs without phonological realization  ¬ª‚üÆblahs‚üØ¬´ (my term) are ‚üÆa subset‚üØ of ‚üÆgraphemes‚üØ ‚üÆnot directly used to represent a basic linguistic unit such as sounds or words‚üØ. ‚üÆÔºøblahsÔºø‚üØ may be ‚üÆÔºøpunctuationÔºø‚üØ, ‚üÆÔºøemojiÔºø‚üØ, etc.  ### punctuation  ¬ª‚üÆPunctuation‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆa subset‚üØ of ‚üÆÔºøblahsÔºø‚üØ ‚üÆused to indicate some sort of interpretation help for text‚üØ. ‚üÆÔºøPunctuationÔºø‚üØ may be roughly divided into ‚üÆÔºøwhitespaceÔºø‚üØ and ‚üÆÔºøvisible punctuationÔºø‚üØ. A ¬ª‚üÆpunctuation mark‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆa single grapheme‚üØ ‚üÆacting as ÔºøpunctuationÔºø‚üØ.  #### visible  ##### enclosing punctuation  ###### brackets  ¬ª‚üÆBrackets‚üØ¬´ are ‚üÆa type of‚üØ ‚üÆpunctuation‚üØ that exist in ‚üÆmirrored opening and closing‚üØ variants and are meant to ‚üÆenclose something‚üØ. ‚üÆÔºøbracketsÔºø‚üØ is ‚üÆthe cover term‚üØ for ‚üÆall of ()‚Äπ‚Ä∫[]{}‚üØ.  ####### names   table:thing|name (non-us)|name (us) ‚üÆ{}‚üØ|‚üÆcurly brackets‚üØ|‚üÆbraces‚üØ or ‚üÆcurly braces‚üØ ‚üÆ‚Äπ‚Ä∫‚üØ|span=2;‚üÆangle brackets‚üØ or ‚üÆchevrons‚üØ ‚üÆ( )‚üØ|‚üÆbrackets‚üØ, ‚üÆround brackets‚üØ|‚üÆparantheses‚üØ ‚üÆ[]‚üØ|‚üÆsquare brackets‚üØ|‚üÆbrackets‚üØ  ‚üÆparentheses‚üØ refer to ‚üÆ()‚üØ in ‚üÆthe US‚üØ, and ‚üÆnothing‚üØ ‚üÆin non-US varieties‚üØ ‚üÆbraces‚üØ refers to ‚üÆ{}‚üØ in ‚üÆthe US‚üØ, and ‚üÆnothing‚üØ in ‚üÆnon-US varieties‚üØ ‚üÆbrackets‚üØ may be used ‚üÆas a cover term‚üØ or refer to ‚üÆ[]‚üØ in ‚üÆthe us‚üØ and ‚üÆ()‚üØ in ‚üÆnon-us varieties‚üØ  Due to ‚üÆUS cultural influence‚üØ, ‚üÆthe US names‚üØ for ()‚Äπ‚Ä∫[]{} may be ‚üÆrecognized in other countries‚üØ.   ##### sentence-ending punctuation  ###### bang  The ‚üÆexlamation point‚üØ may also be called a ‚üÆbang‚üØ. "# writing systems  ¬ª‚üÆA writing system‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆa set of graphemes‚üØ and‚üÆ possible combinations‚üØ used to ‚üÆrepresent linguistic units‚üØ ‚üÆvisually‚üØ. ‚üÆÔºøA writing systemÔºø‚üØ may also be known as a ‚üÆscript‚üØ. ¬ª‚üÆAn orthography‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆa set of rules‚üØ for ‚üÆwriting a languoid‚üØ. ¬ª‚üÆDigraphia‚üØ¬´ is the situation of ‚üÆhaving more than one writing system‚üØ for ‚üÆthe same languoid‚üØ  # ortographic depth  ¬ª‚üÆOrthographic depth‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆthe degree of deviation‚üØ from ‚üÆthe ideal correspondence between‚üØ ‚üÆone character‚üØ and ‚üÆc_;one referred item (morpheme, syllable, phoneme...)‚üØ ¬ªA phonemic orthography¬´ is the special case of ‚üÆÔºøan alphabetÔºø specifically‚üØ ‚üÆhaving no or little orthographic depth‚üØ.  ## !of languages I know  table:orthographic depth|language ‚üÆortographically deep‚üØ|‚üÆEnglish‚üØ ‚üÆortographically shallow‚üØ|‚üÆspanish, japanese (kana)‚üØ  # properties  ## letter case  ¬ª‚üÆLetter case‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆthe distinction between‚üØ ‚üÆuppercase and lowercase letters‚üØ.  ‚üÆuppercase letter‚üØ = ‚üÆcaptial letter‚üØ = ‚üÆmajuscle‚üØ  ‚üÆlowercase letter‚üØ = ‚üÆminuscle‚üØ   ‚üÆuppercase letters‚üØ are ‚üÆgenerally larger than‚üØ ‚üÆthe lowercase letters‚üØ.   ### writing systems  Regarding letter case, writing systems may be ‚üÆÔºøbicameralÔºø‚üØ or ‚üÆc_;ÔºøunicameralÔºø‚üØ. ¬ª‚üÆbicameral‚üØ¬´ writing systems are those that ‚üÆhave a letter case distinction‚üØ. ¬ª‚üÆunicameral‚üØ¬´ writing systems are those that that ‚üÆdon't have a letter case distinction‚üØ.  ### capitalization  ¬ª‚üÆcapitalization‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆwhich letters‚üØ in a word ‚üÆare uppercase‚üØ and ‚üÆc-;which‚üØ ‚üÆare lowercase‚üØ. "# manuscripts  A ¬ª‚üÆmanuscript‚üØ¬´ is a ‚üÆnon-printed‚üØ ‚üÆwritten work‚üØ. "# signs and symptoms  flex-container:‚ú´sm_1_lcJqhlhwT274BKCOXwTO2g.png‚ú´  ¬ª‚üÆSigns and symptoms‚üØ¬´ are the things that allow ‚üÆindividuals‚üØ ‚üÆto notice there is something wrong‚üØ. ¬ª‚üÆpolyunsaturated‚üØ¬´ fats are Ôºøunsaturated fatsÔºø with ‚üÆtwo or more‚üØ ‚üÆC=C double bond(s)‚üØ    ####### cis and trans  ¬ª‚üÆcis and trans‚üØ¬´ are the two ways the ‚üÆstructure of the molecule‚üØ ‚üÆaround the C=C double bond‚üØ may be Only ‚üÆÔºøunsaturated fatsÔºø‚üØ can be ‚üÆÔºøcis or transÔºø‚üØ.  ^Since cis and trans‚üØ fats refer to the structure around the ‚ÅëC=C double bond‚Åë. since an ‚üÆÔºøpolyunsaturated fatty acidÔºø‚üØ ‚üÆcan have more than one double bond‚üØ, it ‚üÆcan be Ôºøboth cis and transÔºø‚üØ   ####### omega  ‚üÆall omega fatty acids‚üØ are ‚üÆunsaturated fatty acids‚üØ. ‚üÆall omega (or maybe just all?) fatty acids‚üØ ‚üÆend in‚üØ ‚üÆa methyl group‚üØ. ‚üÆThe number‚üØ of ‚üÆthe omega fatty acids‚üØ indicates ‚üÆhow many atoms away from the terminal methyl group‚üØ ‚üÆa C=C double bound occurs‚üØ. omega ‚üÆ3 and 6‚üØ are ‚üÆpolyunsaturated fatty acids‚üØ. omega ‚üÆ7 and 9‚üØ are ‚üÆmonunsaturated fatty acids‚üØ.  ######## omega-3  There are ‚üÆthree‚üØ types of ‚üÆomega-3‚üØ fatty acids ‚üÆrelevant to human nutrition‚üØ. The ‚üÆthree types of omega-3 fatty acids relevant to human nutrition‚üØ are ‚üÆALA‚üØ, ‚üÆEPA‚üØ, and ‚üÆDHA‚üØ. of ‚üÆthe three types of omega-3 fatty acids relevant to human nutrition‚üØ, only ‚üÆALA‚üØ is ‚üÆessential‚üØ.  ‚üÆOf the omega-3 fatty acids‚üØ, only ‚üÆALA is essential‚üØ since ‚üÆEPA and DHA‚üØ ‚üÆcan be synthesized from the former‚üØ. ‚üÆEPA/DHA synthesis from ALA‚üØ may be ‚üÆimpaired in aging‚üØ and generally is ‚üÆnot a particularly efficient process‚üØ Due to ‚üÆthe flaws in EPA/DHA synthesis‚üØ ‚üÆmaking sure to consume EPA/DHA‚üØ is probably advisable.  ######## omega-3  ‚üÆthere are a bunch of‚üØ ‚üÆomega-6‚üØ fatty acids, but only ‚üÆlinoleic acid‚üØ is ‚üÆconsidered essential‚üØ ‚á±https://www.hsph.harvard.edu/nutritionsource/what-should-you-eat/fats-and-cholesterol/types-of-fat/omega-3-fats/‚á≤  ######## sources  There are only ‚üÆtwo‚üØ ‚üÆessential‚üØ ‚üÆfatty acids‚üØ. The ‚üÆessential fatty acids‚üØ are one ‚üÆomega-3‚üØ and one ‚üÆc_;omega-6‚üØ fatty acid. ‚üÆALA‚üØ ‚üÆcan be found‚üØ ‚üÆin plant sources‚üØ. ‚üÆALA‚üØ plant sources are mainly ‚üÆseeds and nuts‚üØ. ‚üÆEPA and DHA‚üØ are ‚üÆgenerally not found‚üØ ‚üÆin plant sources‚üØ. ‚üÆalpha-linoleic acid‚üØ ‚üÆcan be found‚üØ ‚üÆin plant sources‚üØ.  ## micro  ### fortification  Food ‚üÆfortification‚üØ = ‚üÆenrichment‚üØ food ‚üÆfortification/enrichment‚üØ is the process of ‚üÆadding micronutrients‚üØ ‚üÆto food‚üØ.   ### genesis  ‚üÆMinerals‚üØ are ‚üÆoriginally found‚üØ in ‚üÆrock, soil, or water‚üØ. ‚üÆMinerals‚üØ ‚üÆfrom the rock/soil/water‚üØ are ‚üÆabsorbed by plants as they grow‚üØ. ‚üÆMinerals‚üØ may also be absorbed by ‚üÆanimals‚üØ by ‚üÆthem licking rocks‚üØ. ‚üÆMinerals‚üØ are ‚üÆpassed up the food chain‚üØ until ‚üÆthey end up in us humans‚üØ. ‚üÆWhat minerals we get‚üØ from ‚üÆa certain plant/animal‚üØ depends on ‚üÆwhich they can store‚üØ, but also ‚üÆwhat the soil/animals further down the food chain had in them‚üØ.   ### vitamins  ‚üÆVitamins‚üØ are produced by being ‚üÆÔºøbiosynthesizedÔºø‚üØ by ‚üÆplants/animals‚üØ. ‚üÆWhich vitamins‚üØ are ‚üÆcontained in food‚üØ depend on ‚üÆwhat the relevant vegetable or animal can biosythesize‚üØ.  Theoretically, all ‚üÆvitamins‚üØ are defined to be ‚üÆessential nutrients‚üØ ‚üÆVitamin D‚üØ can be synthesized if ‚üÆexposed to sunlight (specifically UVB rays)‚üØ Since ‚üÆVitamin D can be synthesized by us‚üØ, it is ‚üÆan exception‚üØ to ‚üÆthe idea that all vitamins are essential‚üØ ¬ª‚üÆvitamers‚üØ¬´ are ‚üÆrelated molecules‚üØ ‚üÆgrouped together into one vitamin‚üØ Most ‚üÆvitamins‚üØ are not ‚üÆsingle molecules‚üØ, but ‚üÆÔºøvitamersÔºø‚üØ. Each ‚üÆvitamin‚üØ is typically used in ‚üÆmultiple reactions‚üØ, and therefore ‚üÆmost have multiple functions‚üØ.  ‚üÆVitamin B12‚üØ is ‚üÆthe only‚üØ ‚üÆvitamin or nutrient‚üØ ‚üÆnot available from plant sources.‚üØ   #### water/fat-soluble  ‚üÆVitamins‚üØ are generally categorized as either ‚üÆwater- or fat soluble‚üØ  The ‚üÆwater-soluble vitamins‚üØ are ‚üÆeasily excreted from the body‚üØ. The ‚üÆfat-soluble vitamins‚üØ can ‚üÆaccumulate in the body‚üØ. ‚üÆHigh storage levels‚üØ is only an issue with those vitamins that ‚üÆcan accumulate in the body (fat-soluble ones)‚üØ, and of those generally only ‚üÆA and D‚üØ. With ‚üÆthe water-soluble vitamins‚üØ, ‚üÆconsistent consumption‚üØ is required.  ### minerals  ‚üÆMinerals‚üØ are ‚üÆmicronutrients‚üØ that ‚üÆare inorganic chemical elements‚üØ   #### macro/trace/ultratrace  ‚üÆMinerals‚üØ as ÔºømicronutrientsÔºø are subcategorized (by ‚üÆamount necessary‚üØ) into ‚üÆmacrominerals‚üØ, ‚üÆtrace minerals‚üØ, and ‚üÆultratrace minerals‚üØ.  There are many ‚üÆultratrace‚üØ elements that have been ‚üÆsuggested as essential‚üØ, such claims are ‚üÆdifficult to reproduce‚üØ.    table:Mineral|Is defined by? ‚üÆMacrominerals‚üØ|‚üÆc+;RDA ‚Ä∫ "# base and superstructure  In marxism, ‚üÆsociety‚üØ ‚üÆconsists of‚üØ two parts, ‚üÆthe ÔºøbaseÔºø‚üØ and ‚üÆthe ÔºøsuperstructureÔºø‚üØ. The ¬ª‚üÆbase‚üØ¬´ consists of ‚üÆforces and relations of production‚üØ. The ¬ª‚üÆsuperstructure‚üØ¬´ consists of ‚üÆculture, institutions, rituals, states, etc.‚üØ In marxism, the ‚üÆbase and superstructure‚üØ ‚üÆreciprocally influence each other‚üØ, but ‚üÆthe base is predominant‚üØ.  # universal equivalent  ¬ª‚üÆthe universal equivalent‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆthe measure‚üØ by which ‚üÆall commodities‚üØ are ‚üÆcompared and exchanged‚üØ  To marx, ‚üÆmoney‚üØ is the thing that eventually takes over the role of ‚üÆthe universal equivalent‚üØ in capitalism.  # value  To ‚üÆmarx‚üØ, the ¬ª‚üÆexchange value‚üØ¬´ of a good is ‚üÆhow it can be exchanged for other commodities‚üØ To ‚üÆmarx‚üØ, the ¬ª‚üÆuse value‚üØ¬´ of a good is ‚üÆits tangible features/physical properties‚üØ that  ‚üÆfulfill human needs/wants‚üØ   form|corresp. value|explanation ‚üÆnatural form‚üØ|‚üÆuse value‚üØ|‚üÆactual tangible form (the actual physical form, e.g. cloth, iron, etc.‚üØ) ‚üÆvalue form‚üØ|‚üÆexchange value‚üØ|‚üÆa good in relation to different commodities (e.g. 20 yards of linen = 1 coat‚üØ)  # labor  ## reproductive labor  ¬ª‚üÆreproductive labor‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆthe work‚üØ that ‚üÆreproduces other labor‚üØ. ‚üÆÔºøReproductive laborÔºø‚üØ is ‚üÆabsolutely necessary‚üØ - without ‚üÆc-;reproductive labor‚üØ, there ‚üÆwould be no labor at all‚üØ. ‚üÆÔºøReproductive laborÔºø‚üØ consists of ‚üÆchores‚üØ and ‚üÆchildrearing‚üØ.  <span class=""cloze-dump"">{{c1::}}{{c2::}}{{c3::}}{{c4::}}{{c5::}}{{c6::}}{{c7::}}{{c8::}}{{c9::}}{{c10::}}{{c11::}}{{c12::}}{{c13::}}{{c14::}}{{c15::}}{{c16::}}{{c17::}}{{c18::}}{{c19::}}{{c20::}}{{c21::}}{{c22::}}{{c23::}}{{c24::}}{{c25::}}{{c26::}}{{c27::}}{{c28::}}{{c29::}}{{c30::}}{{c31::}}{{c32::}}{{c33::}}{{c34::}}{{c35::}}{{c36::}}{{c37::}}{{c38::}}</span>"	misc::econ::marx
"# applied  In some sense, math ‚üÆmay be divided‚üØ into ‚üÆpure mathematics and applied mathematics‚üØ  ¬ª‚üÆpure mathematics‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆthe study of mathematical concepts independent from applications‚üØ  # units   ## units  ‚üÆUnit of measurement‚üØ is often abbreviated ‚üÆunit‚üØ. A ¬ª‚üÆunit (of measurement)‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆa certain ÔºømagnitudeÔºø‚üØ ‚üÆof a given ÔºøquantityÔºø‚üØ. E.g. the metre is a certain magnitude (the length of the path traveled by light in a vacuum ...) of the quantity length ‚üÆÔºøAn unitÔºø‚üØ typically can be ‚üÆidentified by‚üØ its ‚üÆÔºøunit nameÔºø‚üØ and/or ‚üÆÔºøunit symbolÔºø‚üØ. ¬ª‚üÆAn unit name‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆa common noun‚üØ ‚üÆidentifying‚üØ ‚üÆÔºøa unitÔºø‚üØ. ¬ª‚üÆAn unit symbol‚üØ¬´ is a ‚üÆcharacter‚üØ, ‚üÆa few characters‚üØ or ‚üÆa ÔºøexpressionÔºø containing other Ôºøunit symbolsÔºø‚üØ ‚üÆidentifying‚üØ ‚üÆÔºøa unitÔºø‚üØ  ### non-SI  #### imperial ÔºÜ US customary  ¬ª‚üÆImperial units/system‚üØ¬´ are the ‚üÆUnits used in the british empire (after 1826)‚üØ ¬ª‚üÆUnited States customary units‚üØ¬´ are the ‚üÆunits used in the US‚üØ. As ‚üÆan exception‚üØ, the ‚üÆÔºøSI prefix symbolsÔºø‚üØ for ‚üÆ10, 100 and 1000‚üØ are ‚üÆlowercase‚üØ. The ‚üÆÔºøSI prefix symbolsÔºø‚üØ are ‚üÆthe first letter of the prefix name‚üØ, except for ‚üÆdeca (da) and micro (Œº)‚üØ.  #### prefix name  SI prefix name|10‚é¥?‚é¥ ‚üÆexa‚üØ|‚üÆ10‚é¥18‚é¥ = 1000000000000000000‚üØ ‚üÆpeta‚üØ|‚üÆ10‚é¥15‚é¥ = 1000000000000000‚üØ ‚üÆtera‚üØ|‚üÆ10‚é¥12‚é¥ = 1000000000000‚üØ ‚üÆgiga‚üØ|‚üÆ10‚é¥9‚é¥ = 1000000000‚üØ ‚üÆmega‚üØ|‚üÆ10‚é¥6‚é¥ = 1000000‚üØ ‚üÆkilo‚üØ|‚üÆ10‚é¥3‚é¥ = 1000‚üØ ‚üÆhecto‚üØ|‚üÆ10‚é¥2‚é¥ = 100‚üØ ‚üÆdeca‚üØ|‚üÆ10‚é¥1‚é¥ = 10‚üØ √∏|1‚é¥0‚é¥ = 1 ‚üÆdeci‚üØ|‚üÆ10‚é¥‚àí1‚é¥ = 0.1‚üØ ‚üÆcenti‚üØ|‚üÆ10‚é¥‚àí2‚é¥ = 0.01‚üØ ‚üÆmilli‚üØ|‚üÆ10‚é¥‚àí3‚é¥ = 0.001‚üØ ‚üÆmicro‚üØ|‚üÆ10‚é¥‚àí6‚é¥ = 0.000001‚üØ ‚üÆnano‚üØ|‚üÆ10‚é¥‚àí9‚é¥ = 0.000000001‚üØ ‚üÆpico‚üØ|‚üÆ10‚é¥‚àí12‚é¥ = 0.000000000001‚üØ ‚üÆfemto‚üØ|‚üÆ10‚é¥‚àí15‚é¥ = 0.000000000000001‚üØ  ### for binary numbers  ¬ª‚üÆA binary prefix‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆÔºøa unit prefixÔºø‚üØ that ‚üÆis base-2‚üØ. ‚üÆÔºøBinary prefixesÔºø‚üØ are most commonly ‚üÆmultiples of 2‚é¥10‚é¥ = 1024‚üØ. ‚üÆbinary-prefix-symbol‚üØ ::= ‚üÆ‚Äπmetric-prefix-symbol‚Ä∫i‚üØ ‚üÆbinary-prefix-name‚üØ ::= ‚üÆ‚Äπmetric-prefix-name-first-2-chars‚Ä∫bi‚üØ Sometimes, ‚üÆÔºømetri prefixesÔºø‚üØ are used as ‚üÆÔºøbinary prefixesÔºø‚üØ, however this is ‚üÆincorrect usage ‚üØ ‚üÆÔºømetric/decimal prefixesÔºø‚üØ are ‚üÆbase 10‚üØ even when ‚üÆused on bits or bytes‚üØ.  ## SI  ### history/organization  A ‚üÆdecimal‚üØ-based ‚üÆsystem of measurements‚üØ based on ‚üÆthe meter‚üØ was first introduced in ‚üÆfrance‚üØ in ‚üÆthe 1790s‚üØ. ‚üÆÔºøThe metric systemÔºø‚üØ ‚üÆevolved from‚üØ ‚üÆthe french decimalised system.‚üØ ¬ª‚üÆSI‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆa organization/standartization‚üØ of ‚üÆÔºøthe metric systemÔºø‚üØ. ‚üÆÔºøSIÔºø‚üØ is short for ‚üÆInternational System of Units‚üØ ‚üÆSI‚üØ is the ‚üÆnearly universally used‚üØ ‚üÆsystem of measurements‚üØ.   ### base ÔºÜ derived units  The ‚üÆSI‚üØ has ‚üÆ7‚üØ ‚üÆbase units.‚üØ  The ‚üÆ7 Ôºøbase unitsÔºø‚üØ are the units from which ‚üÆall‚üØ ‚üÆÔºøderived unitsÔºø‚üØ ‚üÆcan be derived‚üØ. The ‚üÆ7 Ôºøbase unitsÔºø‚üØ are defined by using seven ‚üÆÔºødefining constantsÔºø‚üØ. the ¬ª‚üÆdefining constants‚üØ¬´ are constants that ‚üÆhave certain exact numerical values‚üØ when ‚üÆexpressed in terms of their SI units‚üØ. e.g. c = 299 792 458 m/s ‚üÆThe SI‚üØ has ‚üÆ22‚üØ ‚üÆÔºøderived unitsÔºø‚üØ. ¬ª‚üÆA derived unit‚üØ¬´ is a unit that ‚üÆcan be expressed as‚üØ ‚üÆa product of Ôºøbase unit(s)Ôºø‚üØ. ‚üÆÔºøDerived unitsÔºø‚üØ may contain ‚üÆfractions‚üØ since ‚üÆc_;a fraction‚üØ is ‚üÆjust multiplication with a thing with negative exponent‚üØ. Within ‚üÆthe 22 Ôºøderived unitsÔºø‚üØ there are ‚üÆ2 unitless units‚üØ, derived via ‚üÆunit elimination‚üØ.  e.g. rad = m/m = 1 From the ‚üÆÔºøbaseÔºø‚üØ and ‚üÆc_;Ôºøderived unitsÔºø‚üØ, ‚üÆother coherent units‚üØ ‚üÆcan be derived‚üØ.   <span class=""cloze-dump"">{{c1::}}{{c2::}}{{c3::}}{{c4::}}{{c5::}}{{c6::}}{{c7::}}{{c8::}}{{c9::}}{{c10::}}{{c11::}}{{c12::}}{{c13::}}{{c14::}}{{c15::}}{{c16::}}{{c17::}}{{c18::}}{{c19::}}{{c20::}}{{c21::}}{{c22::}}{{c23::}}{{c24::}}{{c25::}}{{c26::}}{{c27::}}{{c28::}}{{c29::}}{{c30::}}{{c31::}}{{c32::}}{{c33::}}{{c34::}}{{c35::}}{{c36::}}{{c37::}}{{c38::}}{{c39::}}{{c40::}}{{c41::}}{{c42::}}{{c43::}}{{c44::}}{{c45::}}{{c46::}}{{c47::}}{{c48::}}{{c49::}}{{c50::}}{{c51::}}{{c52::}}{{c53::}}{{c54::}}{{c55::}}{{c56::}}{{c57::}}{{c58::}}{{c59::}}{{c60::}}{{c61::}}{{c62::}}{{c63::}}{{c64::}}{{c65::}}{{c66::}}{{c67::}}{{c68::}}{{c69::}}{{c70::}}{{c71::}}{{c72::}}{{c73::}}{{c74::}}{{c75::}}{{c76::}}{{c77::}}{{c78::}}{{c79::}}{{c80::}}{{c81::}}{{c82::}}{{c83::}}{{c84::}}{{c85::}}{{c86::}}{{c87::}}{{c88::}}{{c89::}}{{c90::}}{{c91::}}{{c92::}}{{c93::}}{{c94::}}{{c95::}}{{c96::}}{{c97::}}{{c98::}}{{c99::}}{{c100::}}{{c101::}}{{c102::}}{{c103::}}{{c104::}}{{c105::}}{{c106::}}{{c107::}}{{c108::}}{{c109::}}{{c110::}}{{c111::}}{{c112::}}{{c113::}}{{c114::}}{{c115::}}{{c116::}}{{c117::}}{{c118::}}{{c119::}}{{c120::}}{{c121::}}{{c122::}}{{c123::}}{{c124::}}{{c125::}}{{c126::}}{{c127::}}{{c128::}}{{c129::}}{{c130::}}{{c131::}}{{c132::}}{{c133::}}{{c134::}}{{c135::}}{{c136::}}{{c137::}}{{c138::}}{{c139::}}{{c140::}}{{c141::}}{{c142::}}{{c143::}}{{c144::}}{{c145::}}{{c146::}}{{c147::}}{{c148::}}{{c149::}}{{c150::}}{{c151::}}{{c152::}}{{c153::}}{{c154::}}{{c155::}}{{c156::}}{{c157::}}{{c158::}}{{c159::}}{{c160::}}{{c161::}}{{c162::}}{{c163::}}{{c164::}}{{c165::}}{{c166::}}{{c167::}}{{c168::}}{{c169::}}{{c170::}}{{c171::}}{{c172::}}{{c173::}}{{c174::}}{{c175::}}{{c176::}}{{c177::}}{{c178::}}{{c179::}}{{c180::}}</span>"	math::applied
"# probability theory  ## random variable  ### discrete or continous  A ‚üÆÔºørandom variableÔºø‚üØ may be ‚üÆÔºøconinousÔºø‚üØ or ‚üÆÔºødiscreteÔºø‚üØ. "# lexicology  ¬ª‚üÆLexicology‚üØ¬´ is the study of ‚üÆÔºølexical unitsÔºø‚üØ. ‚üÆLexical item‚üØ and ‚üÆlexical unit‚üØ are synonyms. ‚üÆÔºøA lexical unitÔºø‚üØ can be ‚üÆa part of a word‚üØ, ‚üÆc_;word‚üØ, or ‚üÆc_;chain of words‚üØ. ¬ª‚üÆA lexeme‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆÔºøa lexical unitÔºø‚üØ that ‚üÆis a single word‚üØ. "# quantitiative linguistics  ¬ª‚üÆQuantitative linguistics‚üØ¬´ is the branch of linguistics that investigates language using ‚üÆquantitative methods‚üØ.  ## linguistic law  ‚üÆÔºøA linguistic lawÔºø‚üØ is a phenomenon in ‚üÆÔºøquantitative linguisticsÔºø‚üØ. ¬ª‚üÆA linguistic law‚üØ¬´ is‚üÆ a statistical regularity‚üØ in ‚üÆthe relationship between scales‚üØ of ‚üÆÔºølinguistic unitsÔºø‚üØ.  ### brevity law  ‚üÆBrevity law‚üØ = ‚üÆzipf's law of abbreviation‚üØ ¬ª‚üÆBrevity law‚üØ¬´ is ‚üÆÔºøa linguistic lawÔºø‚üØ that says ‚üÆthe frequency of use‚üØ of ‚üÆa word‚üØ is ‚üÆinversely proportional‚üØ to ‚üÆc-2;the length‚üØ of ‚üÆa word‚üØ. According to Ôºøbrevity lawÔºø: The ‚üÆshorter a word is‚üØ, the ‚üÆmore often it is used‚üØ; According to Ôºøbrevity lawÔºø: the ‚üÆlonger a word is‚üØ, the ‚üÆless frequently it is used‚üØ ‚üÆÔºøBrevity lawÔºø‚üØ can be found in ‚üÆall investigated (currently 1k languages of 80 language families)‚üØ ‚üÆnatural languages‚üØ , as well as ‚üÆin some other communication systems, such as that of primates.‚üØ ‚üÆÔºøBrevity lawÔºø‚üØ was first described in ‚üÆ1945‚üØ in corpus work on American English by ‚üÆZiph‚üØ.  <span class=""cloze-dump"">{{c1::}}{{c2::}}{{c3::}}{{c4::}}{{c5::}}{{c6::}}{{c7::}}{{c8::}}{{c9::}}{{c10::}}{{c11::}}{{c12::}}{{c13::}}{{c14::}}{{c15::}}{{c16::}}{{c17::}}{{c18::}}{{c19::}}{{c20::}}{{c21::}}{{c22::}}{{c23::}}{{c24::}}{{c25::}}{{c26::}}</span>"	linguistics
